The Slave's Confession to Theft: Validity and General Ruling
Matn ruling on slave's confession with talil reasoning.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on a slave's confession to theft and explain the dual rationale (humanity and property) that validates it.

Core Idea
A slave's confession to theft is valid for hadd due to his humanity, and for property because he is owned.

This lesson addresses a unique Hanafi ruling: a slave's confession to theft leads to both the hadd punishment (cutting of the hand) and the return of stolen property. The school reconciles the slave's dual nature—human and property—to validate the confession. We will explore the text's reasoning and its legal consequences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling
Locate in the text the two consequences of a slave's confession: cutting and return of property.
The text states 'قطع وترد السرقة'.
2
Analyze the first justification: humanity
Note that the confession is valid for hadd and qisas because the slave is a human being (آدمي). This makes him liable for criminal acts against himself.
The text uses 'من حيث إنه آدمي'.
3
Analyze the second justification: property status
Understand that the confession extends to financial liability because the slave is also property (مال). His confession affects the master's right.
The text uses 'من حيث إنه مال'.
4
Examine the absence of suspicion
Recognize that the confession is accepted because it harms the slave (no collusion suspected).
The text says 'لا تهمة في هذا الإقرار لما يشتمل عليه من الإضرار'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولو أقر عبد بسرقة قطع وترد السرقة إلى المسروق منه لأن إقرار العبد على نفسه بالحدود والقصاص صحيح من حيث إنه آدمي ثم يتعدى إلى المالية فيصح من حيث إنه مال، ولأنه لا تهمة في هذ الإقرار لما يشتمل عليه من الإضرار ومثله مقبول على الغير فيقطع العبد . وإذا صح الإقرار بالقطع صح بالمال بناء عليه لأن الإقرار يلاقي حالة البقاء والمال في حالة البقاء تابع فقط حتى تسقط عصمة المال باعتباره ويستوفي القطع بعد استهلاكه أطلق العبد فشمل المأذون والمحجور عليه وخالف محمد في المحجور فقال : لا يقطع وخالفه أبو يوسف، واتفقا على أن المال للمولى. وأطلق في القطع فشمل ما إذا صدقه المولى وكذبه والخلاف فيه فقط. وأطلق في السرقة فشمل القائمة والمستهلكة. وأشار بالرد المقيد لبقائها إلى أنها لو كانت مستهلكة فلا ضمان ويقطع وأشار بالقطع إلى أن العبد كبير إذ لا قطع إلا على مكلف فإذا أقر عبد صغير بسرقة فنر قطع غير أنه إذا كان مأذوناً يرد المال إلى المسروق منه إن كان قائماً، وإن كان هالكاً يضمن، وإن كان محجوراً فإن صدقه المولى يرد المال إلى المسروق منه إن كان قائماً لا ضمان عليه إن كان هالكاً ولا بعد العتق ؛ كذا في فتح القدير. وقيد بالإقرار ليفيد أن السرقة لو ثبتت عليه بالبينة فإنه يقطع بالأولى ويرد المال إلى المسروق منه كما في الذخيرة، لكن يشترط حضرة المولى عند إقامة البينة عند أبي حنيفة ومحمد وقال أبو يوسف ليست بشرط وأما حضرته عند الإقرار بالحدود فليست بشرط اتفاقاً؛ كذا في شرح الطحاوي
And if a slave confessed to theft, he is cut and the stolen property is returned to the one from whom it was stolen, because the confession of a slave against himself regarding hadd and qisas is valid because he is a human being, then it extends to financial matters and is valid because he is property. And because there is no suspicion in this confession due to the harm it contains, and like it is accepted against others, so the slave is cut. And if the confession to cutting is valid, it is valid for the property based on it, because confession meets the state of ongoing existence, and property in the state of ongoing existence is only subsidiary, until the inviolability of the property is removed by it, and the cutting is executed after its consumption. The word 'slave' is general and includes the permitted (ma'dhun) and the restricted (mahjur). Muhammad disagreed regarding the restricted and said he is not cut, and Abu Yusuf disagreed with him. They agreed that the property belongs to the master. And the word 'cutting' is general and includes whether the master believed him or denied him, and the disagreement is only in that. And the word 'theft' is general and includes the existing and the consumed. And he indicated by 'return' restricted to its existence that if it were consumed, there is no guarantee and he is cut. And he indicated by 'cutting' that the slave is adult, since there is no cutting except over a responsible person. So if a young slave confessed to theft, we do not cut, except that if he is permitted, the property is returned to the victim if it exists, and if it is destroyed, he guarantees it. And if he is restricted, then if the master believes him, the property is returned to the victim if it exists, no guarantee if it is destroyed, and not after emancipation. So it is in Fath al-Qadir. And he restricted it to confession to indicate that if the theft were proven against him by evidence, he is cut a fortiori and the property is returned to the victim, as in al-Dhakhira, but the presence of the master is required when the evidence is established according to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad, and Abu Yusuf said it is not a condition. As for his presence at confession regarding hadd, it is not a condition by consensus. So it is in Sharh al-Tahawi.
Source pages p. 109

Commentary
This passage deals with the case of a slave who confesses to theft. The Hanafi school rules that the slave's hand is cut and the stolen property is returned to the owner. The reasoning is that a slave's confession against himself in matters of hadd and qisas is valid because he is a human being; consequently, it is also valid for monetary liability because a slave is property. There is no suspicion of collusion because the confession harms him. The general wording includes both permitted (ma'dhun) and restricted (mahjur) slaves, but Imam Muhammad holds that a restricted slave is not cut, while Abu Yusuf disagrees. Both agree that the property goes to the master. The confession covers both existing and consumed property; if consumed, there is no guarantee but the hand is still cut. The slave must be an adult (mukallaf); a minor's confession does not lead to cutting, though the property is returned. If the theft is proven by evidence rather than confession, the slave is cut a fortiori, but the master's presence during evidence is required according to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad; Abu Yusuf says it is not required. For confession, the master's presence is not required by consensus.

Key Terms
عبد ('abd): A slave, who is considered both a human (for hadd) and property (for financial liability).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, why is a slave's confession to theft valid for the hadd punishment?
A) Because the slave is considered as property and confession affects the master.
B) Because the slave is a human being and his confession against himself is valid.
C) Because the master has given permission.
D) Because the stolen property is returned.

Key Takeaway
A slave's confession to theft is valid both for the hudud punishment (cutting) and for financial restitution, based on his dual nature as human and property.

Special Cases: Restricted Slave, Minor Slave, and Proof by Evidence
Expansion of ruling with intra-madhhab dispute and special conditions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the Hanafi rulings for a restricted slave (mahjur) and a minor slave, and explain the condition of the master's presence when theft is proven by evidence.

Core Idea
Imam Muhammad exempts the restricted slave from cutting; minors are never cut; and evidence requires the master's presence according to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad.

This lesson delves into the nuances of the ruling on a slave's theft. While the general rule is that a slave is cut upon confession, there is an intra-Hanafi disagreement regarding the restricted slave (mahjur). Additionally, the status of a minor slave and the method of proof (confession vs. evidence) introduce further conditions. We will unpack these details from the text.

Guided Path
1
Identify the dispute on mahjur
Locate where the text mentions 'محجور' and the disagreement between Muhammad and Abu Yusuf.
The text says: 'وخالف محمد في المحجور فقال : لا يقطع وخالفه أبو يوسف'.
2
Understand the agreement between Sahibayn
Note that despite the disagreement on cutting, both agree that the property (مال) belongs to the master.
The text says: 'واتفقا على أن المال للمولى'.
3
Analyze the ruling for a minor slave
Look for the condition that the slave must be an adult (كبير) and the consequence for a minor (صغير).
The text states: 'وأشار بالقطع إلى أن العبد كبير إذ لا قطع إلا على مكلف'.
4
Examine evidence vs. confession
Understand that if theft is proven by evidence (بينة), the slave is cut a fortiori, but the master's presence is required according to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad.
The text says: 'وقيد بالإقرار ليفيد أن السرقة لو ثبتت عليه بالبينة فإنه يقطع بالأولى... لكن يشترط حضرة المولى عند إقامة البينة عند أبي حنيفة ومحمد'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

And if a slave confessed to theft, he is cut and the stolen property is returned to the one from whom it was stolen, because the confession of a slave against himself regarding hadd and qisas is valid because he is a human being, then it extends to financial matters and is valid because he is property. Commentary
This passage deals with the case of a slave who confesses to theft. The Hanafi school rules that the slave's hand is cut and the stolen property is returned to the owner. The reasoning is that a slave's confession against himself in matters of hadd and qisas is valid because he is a human being; consequently, it is also valid for monetary liability because a slave is property. Key Terms
محجور (mahjur): A slave under restriction (hajr), meaning he cannot engage in financial transactions without the master's permission.
مأذون (ma'dhun): A slave permitted by the master to engage in trade.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the ruling on cutting a restricted slave (mahjur) who confesses to theft according to Imam Muhammad?
A) He is cut.
B) He is not cut.
C) He is cut only if the master believes him.
D) He is cut only if the property is not consumed.

Key Takeaway
For a slave's theft, the cutting applies to adults and is general, but Imam Muhammad exempts restricted slaves; minors are never cut, and evidence requires the master's presence per Abu Hanifa and Muhammad.

Non-Combination of Cutting and Damages, and Intra-Hanafi Dispute on Partial Theft
Matn ruling on non-combination with intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf on partial theft.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rule that cutting and guarantee do not combine, and explain the intra-Hanafi dispute on whether a thief cut for some thefts must guarantee the remaining items.

Core Idea
Cutting and guarantee never combine; for partial theft, Imam says no guarantee, Sahibayn say guarantee remaining.

This passage establishes a fundamental principle of Hanafi criminal law: the hadd penalty of hand-cutting for theft and financial liability (guarantee) cannot coexist. The proof comes from a hadith and rational reasoning. The passage then delves into an important intra-Hanafi dispute: if a thief is only convicted and cut for some of the stolen items (e.g., from different owners), does he still owe damages for the rest? Abu Hanifa says no – the single penalty covers all due to tadakhul (overlap of punishments). The Sahibayn disagree, requiring guarantee for unproven thefts. Understanding this difference affects how cases of multiple theft are adjudicated.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Main Rule
Read the opening statement: cutting and guarantee do not combine. Note the immediate citation of the hadith and the rational argument that paying damages would retroactively negate the theft, making the cutting invalid.
The rule is absolute: no financial liability after the hand is cut, even if the stolen item is consumed.
2
Analyze the Proof
Study the hadith proof: 'No liability on the thief after his hand is cut.' Check its transmission (Nasai, ahad) and the way it indicates the ruling (wajh al-dalalah). Then follow the rational reasoning: if the thief paid damages, it would imply ownership at the time of taking, thus the theft never occurred and cutting is negated.
The proof is zanni in thubut and dalala, but accepted by the Hanafi school.
3
State the Intra-Hanafi Dispute
The passage presents an ikhtilaf between Imam Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn regarding a thief cut for some thefts. Identify each position: Abu Hanifa says no guarantee for the remaining items; the Sahibayn say he must guarantee all except the one he was cut for.
This is a pure intra-Hanafi difference; both sides are valid within the school.
4
Trace the Reasoning of Each Side
For Abu Hanifa: the hadd obligation for all thefts is one unified right of Allah due to tadakhul; once executed, the entire obligation is fulfilled. For the Sahibayn: the present thief is not a representative for absent thieves (or unproven thefts); each theft requires separate proof and litigation, so only the proven one is covered.
The key conceptual difference is whether the obligation is unitary or itemized.
5
Determine the Practical Consequence (Thamarah)
Consider a scenario: a thief steals from two owners, but only one owner comes forward to testify. The thief is cut. According to Abu Hanifa, the second owner gets nothing; according to the Sahibayn, the thief must pay the value to the second owner.
This is the practical impact: who bears the loss – the victim or the state/community?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله: (ولا يجتمع قطع وضمان وترد العين لو قائمة لقوله عليه السلام لا غرم على السارق بعد ما قطعت يمينه»(۱) ولأن وجوب الضمان ينافي القطع لأنه يتملكه بأداء الضمان مسنداً إلى وقت الأخذ فتبين أنه ورد على ملكه فينتفي القطع، وما يؤدي إلى انتفائه فهو المنتفي أو لأن المحل لا يبقى معصوماً حقاً للعبد إذ ليخاصم فليتأمل اهـ. وقد يقال : يحتمل عوده إليها والكلام فيما يمنع القطع لأنه إذا لم يخاصم لا يقطع وإن لم يهب لاشتراط حضوره عند القطع كما تأمل. قوله : (اقتصر على المقر وإن أنكر فلان) من ضرورة المسروق منه ولا يجتمع قطع وضمان وترد العين لو قائمة ولو قطع لبعض السرقات لا يضمن شيئاً) يعني عند الإمام. وقالا: يضمن كلها إلا التي قطع فيها لأن الحاضر ليس بنائب عن الغائب ولا بد من الخصومة لتظهر السرقة فلم تظهر السرقة من الغائبين فلم يقع القطع لهم فبقيت أموالهم معصومة. وله أن الواجب بالكل قطع واحد حقاً لله تعالى لأن مبنى الحدود على التداخل والخصومة شرط للظهور عند القاضي، أما الوجوب بالجناية وإذا استوفى فالمستوفي كل الواجب ألا ترى أنه يرجع كذا في النسخ بالواو في «وإن) وهو غير ظاهر بل الظاهر حذفها وعبارة منح الغفار «إذا أنكر فلان». قوله: (وكذا لو هلك في يد المشتري منه الخ قال في التتارخانية: ولو أودعه عند غيره فهلك في يد الأصل فيه أن كل موضع لو ضمنه صاحب المال كان له أن يرجع على السارق فليس له أن يضمنه، وفي كل موضع لو ضمنه لا يرجع على السارق فله أن يضمنه والذي يرجع عليه المودع والمستأجر والمرتهن قوله : (ولو) استهلكه فللمالك تضمينه) أي لو استهلكه المشتري أو الموهوب له وفيه أن مقتضى ما قبله عدم التضمين ثم رأيت في النهر قال بعد نقله عبارة المجتبى : فيحتاج إلى الفرق بين الأجنبي والمشتري وفي السراج لو استهلكها غيره بعد القطع كان للمسروق منه أن يضمن المستهلك قيمته اهـ. وهذا بالقواعد أليق وعليه فلا يحتاج إلى الفرق .اهـ. ولكن عبارة السراج
His statement: 'And cutting and guarantee do not combine, and the item is returned if it is existing' due to his statement (peace be upon him): 'No liability on the thief after his right hand is cut.' And because the obligation of guarantee is contrary to cutting, because he owns it by paying the guarantee attached to the time of taking, so it becomes clear that it came upon his ownership, so cutting is negated, and that which leads to its negation is itself negated. Or because the place does not remain inviolable as a right of the slave? Since he does not litigate, let it be considered. And it may be said: It is possible that it returns to her, and the discussion is about what prevents cutting, because if he does not litigate, he is not cut, even if he did not gift, due to the condition of his presence at cutting, as considered. His statement: 'He limited it to the confessor even if so-and-so denied' from the necessity of the victim, and cutting and guarantee do not combine, and the item is returned if it is existing. And if he was cut for some thefts, he does not guarantee anything, meaning according to the Imam. And the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) said: He guarantees all except the one for which he was cut, because the present one is not a representative of the absent one, and litigation is necessary to reveal the theft, so the theft from the absent ones was not revealed, so cutting was not carried out for them, so their properties remain inviolable. And for him (Imam), the obligation by the whole is one cutting as a right of Allah the Exalted, because the foundation of hadd penalties is on overlap, and litigation is a condition for manifestation before the judge. As for the obligation by the crime, when he receives the penalty, he has received the entire obligation. Do you not see that he returns? So it is in the copies with 'waw' in 'wa in' which is not apparent, rather the apparent is to omit it, and the wording of Manh al-Ghaffar is 'if so-and-so denied'. His statement: 'And likewise if it was destroyed in the hand of the buyer etc.' He said in al-TatarKhaniyyah: And if he deposited it with someone else and it was destroyed in his hand, the principle is that wherever the owner would be able to claim from the thief, he cannot claim from the depositary. And wherever he would not be able to claim from the thief, he can claim from the depositary. And the one who can claim back from the depositary, the depositary, the lessee, and the mortgagee. His statement: 'And if he consumed it, the owner can hold him liable' meaning if the buyer or the donee consumed it. And in it, the implication of what preceded is non-liability. Then I saw in al-Nahr that after quoting the phrase of al-Mujtaba, it said: So there is a need to differentiate between the stranger and the buyer. And in al-Siraj, if someone else consumed it after the cutting, the victim can hold the consumer liable for its value. This is more in line with the principles, and accordingly there is no need for differentiation. But the wording of al-Siraj...
Source pages p. 109

Commentary
This passage covers two interrelated topics. First, the principle that cutting the thief's hand and financial guarantee (damages) cannot be combined: after the hand is cut, no monetary liability remains, based on the hadith 'No liability on the thief after his hand is cut.' The commentary provides rational reasoning: if the thief were to pay damages, it would imply ownership retroactively, negating the theft and thus the cutting. Therefore, the cutting takes precedence, and the stolen item is returned if still existing; if consumed, no damages are owed. Second, the passage addresses a dispute between Imam Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) regarding a thief who is cut only for some of the stolen items. According to Imam Abu Hanifa, the cutting covers all thefts, so he does not guarantee the remaining items. The Sahibayn argue that he must guarantee all except the one he was cut for, because the present thief is not a representative for the absent thieves (if the items belong to different people) and litigation is needed to prove each theft. Imam's reasoning is that the hadd penalty is one unified obligation from Allah (the principle of tadakhul/interference of penalties), and once executed, it fulfills the entire obligation. The text also includes a discussion on the liability of third parties who acquire the stolen goods after the cutting: if the thief sold or gave the item, the victim can take it from the buyer or donee if it still exists; if consumed, the victim can hold the consumer liable according to the relied-upon view.

Key Terms
تداخل الحدود (tadakhul al-hudud): The principle that multiple hadd penalties overlap, so one punishment suffices for multiple offenses.
غرم (ghurm): Financial liability or damages that the thief would owe to the victim.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Imam Abu Hanifa, if a thief is cut for some of the stolen items (but not all), what is the ruling on the remaining items?
A) He must guarantee the remaining items
B) He does not guarantee the remaining items
C) He must guarantee only if the remaining items exist
D) He is cut again for the remaining items

Key Takeaway
Cutting and guarantee never combine; for partial theft, the relied-upon Hanafi view (Imam Abu Hanifa) absolves the thief of any remaining financial liability.

Overlapping Hadd Penalties: Liability for Unpunished Thefts
Intra-Hanafi dispute on liability after partial hadd execution

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on overlapping hudud and explain why Abu Hanifa denies liability for thefts not litigated, distinguishing between the condition for the judge's knowledge and the obligation itself.

Core Idea
One hadd punishment suffices for all thefts — tadakhul al-hudud prevents overlapping liability.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq addresses a key intra-Hanafi dispute: when a thief is cut for only some of multiple thefts, is he liable for the rest? Imam Abu Hanifa invokes the principle of tadakhul al-hudud, while the Sahibayn insist that each theft requires individual litigation to establish proof. Understanding this dispute illuminates the Hanafi view on the nature of hadd obligations and the role of litigation.

Guided Path
1
State the ruling in the matn
Identify the core rule: 'If he is cut for some thefts, he does not guarantee anything.' This is the position of Imam Abu Hanifa.
2
Identify the Sahibayn's objection
Abu Yusuf and Muhammad argue that the thief must guarantee all thefts except those for which he was cut, because the present thefts do not represent the absent ones, and litigation is necessary to prove each theft.
Their proof relies on the need for khusumah (litigation) to establish the theft.
3
Analyse Abu Hanifa's response
Abu Hanifa holds that one hadd fulfills the obligation for all thefts based on tadakhul al-hudud (overlapping of penalties). Litigation is only a condition for the judge's knowledge, not for the obligation of the hadd itself.
The distinction is between wujub (obligation) and zuhur (appearance before the judge).
4
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical difference: If a thief is sued for some thefts, Abu Hanifa says no guarantee; the Sahibayn say guarantee for the rest.
This affects whether the thief must pay compensation (damān) for the unlitigated thefts.
5
Note the mufta bihi position
The relied-upon position in the Hanafi school is that of Imam Abu Hanifa: no liability. The matn (Kanz al-Daqa'iq) and its commentaries affirm this.
The Sahibayn's view is recorded but not adopted for fatwa.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (ولو قطع لبعض السرقات لا يضمن شيئاً) يعني عند الإمام. وقالا : يضمن كلها إلا التي قطع فيها لأن الحاضر ليس بنائب عن الغائب ولا بد من الخصومة لتظهر السرقة فلم تظهر السرقة من الغائبين فلم يقع القطع لهم فبقيت أموالهم معصومة. وله أن الواجب بالكل قطع واحد حقاً لله تعالى لأن مبنى الحدود على التداخل والخصومة شرط للظهور عند القاضي، أما الوجوب بالجناية وإذا استوفى فالمستوفي كل الواجب ألا ترى أنه يرجع كذا في النسخ بالواو في «وإن) وهو غير ظاهر بل الظاهر حذفها وعبارة منح الغفار «إذا أنكر فلان».
He said: (And if he is cut for some of the thefts, he does not guarantee anything) meaning according to Imam Abu Hanifa. And they (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) said: He guarantees all except those for which he was cut, because the present does not represent the absent, and litigation is necessary for the theft to be proven, so the theft of the absent ones was not proven, so the cutting did not occur for them, so their properties remain protected. And for him (Abu Hanifa) that the obligatory due to all is a single cut as a right of Allah because the basis of hudud is overlapping (tadakhul), and litigation is a condition for its appearing before the judge, but as for the obligation due to the crime, when he has fulfilled it, he has fulfilled the entire obligation. Do you not see that he returns? Similarly in the copies with the waw in 'wa in' which is not apparent, rather the apparent is its deletion, and the phrase of Manh al-Ghaffar: 'If so-and-so denies'.
Source pages p. 110

Commentary
This is an intra-Hanafi dispute. The matn states that if a thief is cut for only some of multiple thefts, he does not guarantee the value of the rest. This is the position of Imam Abu Hanifa. The Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) disagree, arguing that the absent thefts were not litigated, so their property remains protected and must be guaranteed. Abu Hanifa's proof is the principle of tadakhul (overlapping) of hudud: one hadd punishment fulfills the obligation for all similar crimes. Litigation is only a condition for the judge's knowledge, not for the obligation itself. The text also notes a variant reading without the conjunction 'wa'.

Key Terms
تداخل الحدود (tadakhul al-hudud): The Hanafi principle that one hadd punishment suffices for multiple crimes of the same type, preventing repetition of punishment.
الخصومة (al-khusumah): Legal litigation or claim before a judge, necessary to establish the theft for the purpose of hadd.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Imam Abu Hanifa, if a thief is cut for only one of multiple thefts, what is his liability for the rest?
A) He must guarantee all the other thefts.
B) He does not guarantee anything for the rest.
C) He guarantees only if the value is above nisab.
D) He guarantees only if the owner sues for each theft.

Key Takeaway
In the Hanafi school, one hadd punishment covers all similar crimes due to the principle of tadakhul al-hudud, making the thief not liable for unlitigated thefts.

Ruling on Liability when Stolen Item is Consumed by Buyer
Determining liability when stolen item is consumed by buyer after amputation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the owner's right to claim compensation from a buyer who consumes a stolen item after the thief's hand is cut, and justify it using the Hanafi principle of recourse.

Core Idea
The owner may claim directly from the consumer, regardless of recourse, after the thief's amputation.

When a thief's hand is cut, the ownership of the stolen item is transferred to the original owner. If the item perishes in the hands of a buyer or donee, the question of liability arises. This passage clarifies that if the buyer consumes the item, the owner can claim its value from him directly, without needing to pursue the thief.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario
The passage deals with a stolen item that has been sold or given as a gift, and then perishes or is consumed after the thief's hand has been cut. Determine the parties involved: owner, thief, buyer/donee.
2
State the general principle from al-Tatarkhaniyya
The owner can claim from a possessor only if that possessor has no recourse against the thief. This applies to depositary, lessee, pledgee — they have recourse.
The principle limits the owner's direct claim against innocent possessors who can recover from the thief.
3
Analyze the issue of consumption by buyer/donee
The text notes a potential contradiction: the previous view might suggest no liability for the buyer. However, al-Siraj states the owner may claim from the consumer. Why? Because consumption extinguishes the item, and the consumer is directly liable.
Consumption (istihlak) changes the analysis because the buyer has made the item his own by using it.
4
Apply the principle to the buyer
The buyer who consumes the item does not have recourse against the thief? Actually, he might, but the principle from al-Siraj overrides the distinction between stranger and buyer. The owner can always claim from the consumer.
This removes the need to check whether the buyer has recourse.
5
Conclusion and mufta bihi
The relied-upon view is that the owner can claim from the consumer, as per al-Siraj and al-Nahr. No distinction needed.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (وكذا لو هلك في يد المشتري منه الخ قال في التتارخانية: ولو أودعه عند غيره فهلك في يد الأصل فيه أن كل موضع لو ضمنه صاحب المال كان له أن يرجع على السارق فليس له أن يضمنه، وفي كل موضع لو ضمنه لا يرجع على السارق فله أن يضمنه والذي يرجع عليه المودع والمستأجر والمرتهن قوله : (ولو) استهلكه فللمالك تضمينه) أي لو استهلكه المشتري أو الموهوب له وفيه أن مقتضى ما قبله عدم التضمين ثم رأيت في النهر قال بعد نقله عبارة المجتبى : فيحتاج إلى الفرق بين الأجنبي والمشتري وفي السراج لو استهلكها غيره بعد القطع كان للمسروق منه أن يضمن المستهلك قيمته اهـ. وهذا بالقواعد أليق وعليه فلا يحتاج إلى الفرق .اهـ. ولكن عبارة السراج
He said: (And similarly if it perishes in the hand of the buyer, etc.) He said in al-Tatarkhaniyya: And if he deposited it with another and it perished in his hand, the principle is that whenever the owner could sue the depositary and then have recourse against the thief, he cannot sue the depositary; but whenever he cannot have recourse against the thief, he can sue. The depositary, lessee, and pledgee have recourse (against the thief). He said: (And if) the buyer or donee consumes it, the owner may claim its value from him. It implies that the preceding view was no liability; then I saw in al-Nahr after quoting al-Mujtaba: a distinction is needed between a stranger and a buyer. In al-Siraj: If someone else consumes it after the cut, the owner may claim the value from the consumer. This is more consistent with the principles, so no distinction is needed. But the wording of al-Siraj...
Source pages p. 110

Commentary
This passage discusses the liability when a stolen item perishes after the thief's hand has been cut. The principle stated from al-Tatarkhaniyya is that the owner may claim from the person in possession only if that person has no recourse against the thief. For example, a depositary, lessee, or pledgee who pays the owner can then recover from the thief. If the buyer or donee consumes the item, the owner can claim its value from them. The text notes a potential contradiction and then harmonizes it: the owner may always claim from the consumer, as per al-Siraj, which is more in line with the principles.

Key Terms
الاستهلاك (al-istihlak): Consumption of the stolen item by the buyer or donee, extinguishing its physical existence.
الرجوع (al-ruju'): The right of the possessor who compensated the owner to seek reimbursement from the thief.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) No, because the owner must claim from the thief only.
B) Yes, if the buyer consumed the item.
C) Only if the buyer had no recourse against the thief.
D) Only if the buyer knew it was stolen.

Key Takeaway
After amputation, if a buyer or donee consumes the stolen item, the owner may claim its value directly from the consumer, as per al-Siraj.

Cutting the Stolen Item Inside the House
Whether amputation is due if the thief cuts the stolen cloth inside the house before taking it out.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on amputation when a thief cuts the stolen item inside the house and explain the exception for destructive cutting (itlaf).

Core Idea
Amputation is due if the thief cuts inside, unless the cut destroys the item, which is disputed.

This lesson examines a nuanced scenario in the law of theft: what happens if a thief damages the stolen property while still inside the house and then takes it out? Does the damage create a doubt that precludes amputation? The Hanafi school has a clear position with a well-known internal disagreement. Let's explore the ruling and its proof.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and the ruling
The act is cutting the stolen item inside the house and then taking it out. Determine whether amputation is wajib or not.
The passage mentions both the cloth-cutting and sheep-slaughter examples.
2
Locate the underlying proof
The proof is based on the completion of theft: taking out (ikhraj) is the constitutive act. Cutting does not create ownership.
No specific Quran/hadith is quoted; the ruling is derived from general principles.
3
Understand the exception
If the cutting is so severe that it amounts to destruction (itlaf), the thief must pay the value, and amputation is dropped.
Itlaf occurs when more than half the value is lost, according to Hanafi terminology.
4
Note the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf
Abu Yusuf holds that any cutting inside creates a shubha (doubt) and precludes amputation, even without itlaf.
The relied-upon view is the majority position of Abu Hanifa and Muhammad.
5
Evaluate the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical difference: under the majority, amputation is due; under Abu Yusuf, it is not.
The mufta bihi is amputation due, based on the matn and later tarjih.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولو شق ما سرقه في الدار ثم أخرجه قطع ولو سرق شاة فذبحها فأخرجها لا قطع عليه لأن السرقة تمت على اللجم ولا قطع فيه. أطلقه فشمل ما إذا ساوت نصاباً بعد الذبح، وقيد بعدم القطع لأنه يضمن قيمتها للمسروق منه. قوله : (ولو صنع المسروق دراهم أو دنانير قطع (وردها أي لو صنع السارق وهذا عند أبي حنيفة. وقالا : لا سبيل للمسروق منه عليها وأصله في الغصب فهذه صنعة متقومة ليست صريحة في التسوية بل ظاهرها ذلك. وفي التتارخانية عن المنتقى : قطع السارق والعين قائمة في يده وقد غيبه ثم استهلکه رجل آخر فلا ضمان على المستهلك وفيها عن المحيط : وإن كان المشتري أو الموهوب له فللمالك أن يضمنه ثم يرجع المشتري على السارق بالثمن لا بالقيمة. وفيها عن شرح الطحاوي: ولو قطع ثم استهلكه غيره كان للمسروق منه أن يضمنه قيمته. قوله: (وعلى هذا إذا كان العين كلها لواحد كذا في بعض النسخ وفي بعضها النصب» بدل «العين» وهي
And if he cuts what he stole inside the house and then takes it out, amputation is due. And if he steals a sheep and slaughters it and takes it out, no amputation is due because the theft was completed on (the animal) but no amputation due on it. It is absolute so includes if its value after slaughter reaches nisab, and it is restricted by no amputation because he guarantees its value to the owner. He said: (And if he makes the stolen into dirhams or dinars, amputation is due and he returns them) i.e. if the thief makes that. This is according to Abu Hanifa. And they (Sahibayn) said: The owner has no recourse on them; its origin is in usurpation, so this is a valuable craftsmanship, not explicit in equal valuation, rather its apparent is that. And in al-Tatarkhaniyya from al-Muntaqa: The thief's hand is cut and the item remains in his hand and he has concealed it, then another person consumes it, so no liability on the consumer. And in it from al-Muhit: If the buyer or donee, the owner can claim from him, then the buyer recovers from the thief the price, not the value. And in it from Sharh al-Tahawi: If he cuts and then someone else consumes it, the owner may claim its value from him. He said: (And upon this, if the item is all for one person, so in some copies, and in some 'al-nasb' instead of 'al-'ayn' and it is...
Source pages p. 111

Commentary
This passage contains several rulings. First, if the thief cuts the stolen cloth inside the house and then takes it out, amputation is still due, even if the cloth loses value. This is because the act of taking (ikhraj) constitutes the theft. However, if the cutting is so severe that it amounts to destruction (itlaf), then the thief owns the cloth after paying its value, and amputation is not due. Second, if the thief slaughters a stolen sheep and takes out the meat, no amputation is due because the theft is considered complete on the animal, and slaughtering destroys it; the thief must guarantee its value. Third, if the thief melts stolen coins into a new form, Imam Abu Hanifa says amputation is due and the new coins are returned to the owner, but the Sahibayn say the owner has no recourse because the craftsmanship changes ownership, similar to usurpation. The text also discusses liability when a third party consumes the stolen item after the hand is cut.

Key Terms
الشق الفاحش (al-shaqq al-fahish): A severe cut that reduces both the substance and significant benefit of the cloth, amounting to destruction (itlaf).
الإتلاف (al-itlaf): Complete destruction of the stolen item, such that the thief must pay full value and may become owner, precluding amputation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Cutting the item at all.
B) Cutting that amounts to itlaf (destruction).
C) Cutting after taking it out.
D) Cutting a sheep.

Key Takeaway
Amputation is due for a thief who cuts the stolen item inside the house unless the cutting amounts to destruction (itlaf), and even then, it is disputed.

Tearing the Stolen Item Inside the House: Cutting or Not?
Intra-Hanafi dispute on cutting for tearing inside

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on cutting when a thief tears the stolen item inside the house and explain the intra-Hanafi dispute between Abu Hanifa/Muhammad and Abu Yusuf.

Core Idea
Tearing the stolen item inside the house does not create ownership doubt sufficient to prevent hadd according to the majority Hanafis.

This lesson examines a nuanced ruling from the Book of Theft in Al-Bahr al-Raiq. The base text states that if a thief tears the stolen item inside the house and then takes it out, the hand is cut. However, Imam Abu Yusuf disagrees, arguing that an excessive tear creates a guarantee (ḍamān) that implies ownership, generating a legal doubt (shubha) that averts the hadd. We will dissect the proofs of both sides and determine the mufta bihi position.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and base ruling
The act: thief tears the stolen item inside the house, then takes it out. The base text (Kanz al-Daqaiq) rules that cutting applies. Note that the tear is absolute, including slight and excessive.
The base ruling does not distinguish between slight and excessive tears initially.
2
State the intra-Hanafi dispute
Abu Yusuf holds that no cutting applies when the tear is excessive, due to shubha of ownership. Abu Hanifa and Muhammad hold that cutting still applies.
No inter-school dispute; only within the Hanafi school.
3
Analyze Abu Yusuf's proof
Abu Yusuf analogizes to a buyer who steals a sold item with a seller's option: since the buyer has a right (option), the hadd is averted. Here, the excessive tear obligates compensation, which according to him creates ownership (to avoid double compensation), thus generating a doubt.
The key is whether guarantee necessarily implies ownership.
4
Analyze the majority response
Abu Hanifa and Muhammad argue that taking the item only creates a cause for guarantee, not ownership. Ownership is only a necessary consequence of guarantee to prevent two compensations in one ownership, but the act of taking does not itself create ownership. The tear does not create doubt; it is like the act of taking itself.
They distinguish sale (which is designed for ownership) from theft (which is not).
5
Determine the mufta bihi and practical consequence
The relied-upon position (mufta bihi) is cutting, following Abu Hanifa and Muhammad. However, later scholars like al-Khabbazi and Fath al-Qadir favored compensation without cutting, creating some divergence. The practical difference: according to the majority, the thief is cut; according to Abu Yusuf, he is not cut but must compensate.
Also note the conditions: if the tear is destruction (more than half value lost), then no cutting by consensus, and the thief owns the item with full compensation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب السرقة
يضمن شيئاً ولو شق ما سرقه في الدار ثم أخرجه قطع ولو سرق شاة فذبحها الكل فيقع عن الكل، وعلى هذا الخلاف إذا كان العين كلها لواحد وسرقها منه مراراً فخاصم في البعض ولذا أطلق المصنف فشمل ما إذا كان الكل لواحد كما شمل ما إذا كان المتعدد وحضر الكل وقطع بالبعض أو حضر البعض فقط قوله : (ولو شق ما سرقه في الدار ثم أخرجه (قطع) كما إذا سرق ثوباً فشقه نصفين ثم أخرجه، وعن أبي يوسف عدمه لشبهة الملك فإن الخرق الفاحش يوجب القيمة فيملك المضمون وصار كالمشتري إذا سرق مبيعاً فيه خيار البائع. ولهما أن الأخذ وضع سبباً للضمان لا للملك وإنما يثبت الملك ضرورة إذ الضمان كيلا يجتمع البدلان في ملك واحد ونفسه لا يورث الشبهة كنفس الآخذ، وكما إذا سرق البائع مبيعاً باعه بخلاف ما ذكر لأن البيع وضع لإفادة الملك. أطلق الشق فشمل ما إذا كان فاحشاً أو يسيراً لكن لا خلاف في القطع إذا كان يسيراً لعدم وجوب الضمان وترك الثوب عليه وإنما يضمن النقصان القطع ، وكذا إذا كان الخرق فاحشاً. وصحح الخبازي عدم مع وجوب الضمان لأنه لا يجتمع مع القطع. ورجح في فتح القدير الضمان تبعاً لقاضيخان وقال : إنه الحق لوجوب الضمان بالخرق قبل الإخراج واختلفوا في الفرق بين واليسير، والصحيح أن الفاحش ما يفوت به بعض العين وبعض المنفعة، واليسير ما لا يفوت به شيء من المنفعة بل يتعيب به فقط . ويرد على المصنف رحمه الله شيئان: أحدهما أن القطع مقيد بما إذا اختار تضمين النقصان وأخذ الثوب وإن اختار تضمين القيمة وترك النوب عليه فلا قطع اتفاقاً لأنه ملكه مستنداً إلى وقت الأخذ وقد يجاب بأن هذا الاختيار مسقط للقطع بعد وجوبه فصار كما إذا وهبه العين بل أولى لاستناده واقتصار الهبة وكلام المصنف الوجوب . ثانيهما أن الشق لو كان إتلافاً فله تضمين القيمة من غير خيار ويملك السارق الثوب ولا يقطع وحد الإتلاف أن ينقص أكثر من نصف القيمة، فلو قال المصنف قطع ما لم يكن إتلافاً لكان أولى ولا بد أن تكون قيمة الثوب نصاباً بعد الشق
Book of Theft. He guarantees something, and if he tears what he stole in the house then takes it out, he is cut. And if he stole a sheep and slaughtered it, the whole [ruling] applies for the whole. And upon this dispute when the property is all for one person and he stole it repeatedly and disputed in some, therefore the author [مصنف] gave an absolute ruling that includes when the whole is for one person as it includes when there are multiple and all are present and cut for some or only some are present. His statement: (And if he tears what he stole in the house then takes it out, he is cut) as if he stole a cloth and tore it in half then took it out. And from Abu Yusuf: no cut due to doubt of ownership, because the excessive tear necessitates compensation so he owns what is guaranteed, and it becomes like a buyer who stole a sold item with a seller's option. And for them (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad) that taking places a cause for guarantee, not for ownership; ownership is only established necessarily because guarantee prevents two compensations in one ownership, and the tear itself does not create doubt like the taker himself, and like when the seller stole a sold item he sold, contrary to what was mentioned because sale is placed for ownership. He made the tearing absolute, so it includes whether excessive or slight, but there is no dispute about cutting if it is slight because no compensation is obligatory and the cloth remains on him, he only guarantees the reduction. And likewise if the tear is excessive. Al-Khabbazi authenticated no cut with obligatory compensation because it does not combine with cutting. And in Fath al-Qadir, he favored compensation following Qadikhan and said it is the truth because compensation is obligatory by the tear before taking out, and they differed on the distinction between excessive and slight, and the correct is that excessive is what causes loss of part of the property and part of its benefit, while slight is what does not cause loss of any benefit but only defects it. Two objections are raised against the author: first, that cutting is conditional upon his choosing to guarantee the defect and take the cloth; if he chooses to guarantee the value and leave the cloth, there is no cutting by consensus because he becomes owner from the time of taking. It may be answered that this choice drops the cutting after it becomes obligatory, so it becomes as if he gave the item as a gift, even more so because it is based on and limited to the gift, and the author's statement is about obligation. Second, that if the tear was destruction, he can guarantee the value without option and the thief owns the cloth and is not cut, and the limit of destruction is that it reduces more than half the value. If the author had said 'cut unless it was destruction' it would be better, and the value of the cloth must be a nisab after the tear.

Commentary
This passage is from the commentary on Kanz al-Daqaiq, discussing the ruling of cutting the hand of a thief who tears the stolen item inside the house before taking it out. The base text states that if he tears it inside and then takes it out, he is cut. The commentator expands the ruling. There is an intra-Hanafi dispute: Abu Yusuf holds that no cutting applies because the excessive tear creates a guarantee (ضمان) which transfers ownership to the thief, creating a doubt (شبهة) that prevents the hadd. He analogizes it to a buyer who steals an item with an option for the seller; since the buyer has a right, cutting is averted. Abu Hanifa and Muhammad argue that taking the item only creates a cause for guarantee, not ownership; ownership is established only as a necessary consequence of guarantee to avoid two compensations belonging to one owner. The tear itself does not create a doubt, just as the act of taking does not. They distinguish from sale, which is intended to transfer ownership. The commentator also explains that the tear is absolute, including both slight and excessive. There is no dispute on cutting when the tear is slight because no compensation is due. For excessive tear, al-Khabbazi said no cutting with obligatory compensation, which is rejected in Fath al-Qadir because compensation is due before taking out. The correct definition of excessive tear is one that causes loss of part of the property and benefit; slight tear does not. Two objections to the author: (1) Cutting may be avoided if the thief chooses to take the cloth and compensate only the defect; consensus says if he chooses to pay full value and leave the cloth, no cutting. This is answered that the choice drops cutting after obligation. (2) If the tear is destruction (إتلاف), then the thief pays full value and owns the cloth without cutting; the destruction threshold is reduction of more than half the value. The commentator suggests the author should have conditioned cutting on non-destruction. Also, the cloth's value after the tear must be nisab.

Key Terms
شق (shaqq): To tear or split the stolen item; the focus is on whether the action creates a doubt preventing hadd.
فاحش (fāhish): Excessive tear that causes loss of part of the property and its benefit, as opposed to slight tear that only defects.
ضمان (ḍamān): Guarantee or compensation; the thief becomes liable for the value due to the tear.
إتلاف (itlāf): Destruction: reduction of more than half the value of the item; in such a case, the thief pays full compensation and takes ownership without cutting.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad, why does tearing the stolen item inside the house not prevent cutting?
A) Because the tear is always slight
B) Because taking only causes guarantee, not ownership
C) Because the thief owns the item by tearing
D) Because cutting is not required for tears

Key Takeaway
The majority Hanafi position holds that tearing the stolen item inside the house does not create a legal doubt (shubha) sufficient to avert the hadd of cutting, because taking only establishes a guarantee, not ownership.

Stealing and Slaughtering a Sheep: No Hadd
Ruling on cutting when thief slaughters a stolen sheep before taking it out

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on a thief who slaughters a stolen sheep inside the house and identify the reason why the hadd of cutting does not apply.

Core Idea
Slaughtering changes the stolen item to meat; no cutting for meat.

The hadd punishment for theft is severe, but not every taking triggers the cutting of the hand. This lesson examines a specific scenario: a thief steals a sheep, slaughters it while still on the owner's property, and then takes the meat out. The Hanafi school rules that no cutting is required. We will explore the legal reasoning behind this exception and its limits.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Act and Ruling
What is the act? Theft of a sheep followed by slaughter inside the house, then removal. The ruling is 'no cutting' (la qat'a).
The ruling is absolute, covering cases where meat reaches nisab.
2
Locate the Dalil
The proof is aqli (rational): the theft is completed on meat, and hadd is not established for meat. Slaughtering alters the legal status of the animal.
The source does not cite a verse or hadith; it relies on the legal maxim that hadd is for items that can be preserved and returned.
3
Trace the Wajh al-Dalalah
The theft is 'completed' (tammat) on the meat at the moment of slaughter, not on the live animal. Since the hadd of cutting applies only to specific items (e.g., a live animal that can be taken out), meat is excluded.
This is based on the principle that cutting and guarantee (damān) do not combine.
4
Note the Scope
The ruling includes cases where the meat itself reaches the nisab value. Even then, no cutting. The thief must pay compensation (damān).
The ruling is general (mutlaq) and not restricted by value.
5
Evaluate the Practical Consequence
The thief avoids the hadd but remains liable for the value of the sheep. This preserves the owner's right while upholding the limits of the punishment.
This shows the Hanafi emphasis on precise conditions for hadd.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (ولو سرق شاة فذبحها فأخرجها (لا أي لا قطع عليه لأن السرقة تمت على اللحم ولا قطع فيه. أطلقه فشمل ما إذا ساوت نصاباً بعد الذبح، وقيد بعدم القطع لأنه يضمن قيمتها للمسروق منه.
His statement: (And if he stole a sheep and slaughtered it and then took it out, no cut) i.e., no cutting is upon him because the theft is completed on the meat and there is no cutting in it. He made it absolute, so it includes when it equals nisab after slaughter, and he restricted it with no cutting because he guarantees its value to the owner.
Source pages p. 111

Commentary
This is a ruling on the theft of a sheep that the thief slaughters inside the house and then takes out. The commentator explains that no cutting applies because the theft is completed on the meat, and the hadd of cutting is not applicable to meat. This ruling is absolute, including cases where the meat itself reaches the nisab threshold. The reason for no cutting is that the thief must guarantee (pay compensation for) the value of the sheep to its owner, and cutting and guarantee do not combine. The legal reasoning is that slaughtering changes the status of the item from a living animal to carrion, which is not a subject of the hadd. The theft is considered complete on the meat, which is not protected by the hadd punishment. The thief remains liable for the value.

Key Terms
شاة (shāh): A sheep or goat; the ruling applies to any such animal.
ذبح (dhabḥ): Slaughtering; an act that transforms the animal into carrion, removing it from the category of items that trigger hadd.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why is the thief not cut when he slaughters a stolen sheep inside the house and then takes it out?
A) Because the sheep was not taken out alive
B) Because the theft is completed on meat and there is no cutting for meat
C) Because the thief owns the meat by slaughtering
D) Because the sheep's value is less than nisab

Key Takeaway
Slaughtering a stolen animal before removal changes the theft to meat, which is exempt from hadd, but compensation is still due.

Manufacturing Stolen Dirhams or Dinars
Intra-Hanafi dispute on cutting and ownership when thief manufactures stolen money

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi positions on manufacturing stolen money and explain the thamarah al-khilaf between Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn.

Core Idea
Abu Hanifa: cut and return; Sahibayn: no cut, owner entitled to compensation only.

The Hanafi school has an internal disagreement on whether a thief who manufactures stolen coins into a different form is still subject to the hadd of cutting and whether the manufactured item must be returned. This lesson examines the positions of Abu Hanifa and his two disciples (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani), their proofs, and the practical consequences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Hanafi position
Determine what Abu Hanifa rules regarding the thief and the manufactured item.
Abu Hanifa holds that the thief is cut and the item is returned to the owner.
2
Identify the Sahibayn position
Find the ruling of Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani.
They say the owner has no access to the manufactured item; the owner only claims compensation.
3
Trace the dalil
Understand the basis for the Sahibayn's view: analogy (qiyas) to usurpation (ghasb).
In usurpation, transforming property creates a new ownership right subject to value compensation.
4
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
What is the practical difference in outcome?
Under Abu Hanifa, the owner receives the specific manufactured item; under Sahibayn, only monetary compensation.
5
Note the mufta bihi
Which position is relied upon in later Hanafi fatwa?
The text does not specify tarjih, but Abu Hanifa's view is presented as the matn and commonly followed.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (ولو صنع المسروق دراهم أو دنانير قطع (وردها أي لو صنع السارق وهذا عند أبي حنيفة. وقالا : لا سبيل للمسروق منه عليها وأصله في الغصب فهذه صنعة متقومة
ليست صريحة في التسوية بل ظاهرها ذلك. وفي التتارخانية عن المنتقى : قطع السارق والعين قائمة في يده وقد غيبه ثم استهلکه رجل آخر فلا ضمان على المستهلك وفيها عن المحيط : وإن كان المشتري أو الموهوب له فللمالك أن يضمنه ثم يرجع المشتري على السارق بالثمن لا بالقيمة. وفيها عن شرح الطحاوي: ولو قطع ثم استهلكه غيره كان للمسروق منه أن يضمنه قيمته. قوله: (وعلى هذا إذا كان العين كلها لواحد كذا في بعض النسخ وفي بعضها النصب» بدل «العين» وهي
His statement: (And if he manufactures the stolen dirhams or dinars, he is cut and they are returned) i.e., if the thief manufactures, and this is according to Abu Hanifa. And both (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) said: The owner has no access to them, and its origin is in usurpation, so this is a valuable manufacture not explicitly indicating equalization, but its apparent meaning is that. And in al-TatarKhaniyya from al-Muntaqa: If the thief is cut and the property remains in his hand and he had hidden it, then another person destroys it, there is no liability on the destroyer. And in it from al-Muhit: If it is a buyer or donee, the owner can hold him liable, then the buyer recovers the price from the thief, not the value. And in it from Sharh al-Tahawi: If he is cut and then another destroys it, the owner can hold him liable for its value. His statement: (And upon this if the entire property is for one person) so in some copies, and in some 'al-nasab' instead of 'al-ayn', and it is...
Source pages p. 111

Commentary
This passage discusses the ruling when a thief manufactures stolen gold or silver coins into something else. According to Abu Hanifa, the thief is cut (had applied) and the manufactured item must be returned to the original owner. Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (the Sahibayn) hold that the owner cannot reclaim the item; instead, the owner can only claim compensation from the thief. The difference stems from whether the manufacturing is considered a change that transfers ownership. Abu Hanifa sees that the act of manufacturing does not annul the owner's right to the specific property, especially before cutting. The Sahibayn analogize to usurpation (ghasb) where the manufacturer gains ownership subject to value compensation. The text also references various rulings from other works: if the thief is cut and then someone else destroys the item, there is no liability on the destroyer; if the item is bought or gifted, the owner can hold the buyer/gift recipient liable, who then recoups from the thief. The passage ends with a note on a variant reading in the matn (''ayn'' vs. ''nasab'').

Key Terms
صنع (ṣanaʿa): To manufacture or transform stolen money into a different form (e.g., making coins into jewelry).
متقومة (mutaqawwima): Having value (مال متقوم); the manufactured item has market value.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Hanifa, what happens when a thief manufactures stolen dirhams into jewelry?
A) The thief is cut and the jewelry is returned to the owner
B) The thief is cut but the jewelry belongs to the thief with compensation
C) The thief is not cut and the jewelry is returned
D) The thief is not cut and the owner gets compensation only

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, manufacturing stolen money does not automatically transfer ownership; Abu Hanifa prioritizes the original property right over the transformational act.

Dyeing the Stolen Cloth: Red vs Black (Intra-Hanafi Dispute)
Intra-Hanafi ruling on cutting and return for red/black dye

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the Hanafi rulings for red and black dye on stolen cloth and explain the intra-Hanafi disagreements regarding return and guarantee.

Core Idea
For red dye, the thief keeps the cloth; for black, he returns it—the color determines whether the addition is an increase or a defect.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Raiq delves into a nuanced intra-Hanafi dispute about the legal consequences when a thief dyes a stolen cloth before being cut. The key variable is the color of the dye: red is considered an increase that transfers ownership to the thief, while black is a defect that preserves the owner's right. The commentator explains the positions of Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, and Muhammad al-Shaybani, along with the ruling on cutting.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three rulings stated in the text
The passage explicitly discusses three rulings: (1) obligation of cutting, (2) non-return of the cloth when dyed red, and (3) no guarantee (damān) when red-dyed cloth is destroyed. For black dye, the ruling is return.
2
Understand the basis for cutting
Cutting is wajib because the theft was of the white cloth, which the thief never owned. The dyed cloth is a new object owned by the thief, analogous to grinding stolen wheat into flour.
The analogy (qiyas) with wheat/flour is key.
3
Distinguish the Sahibayn vs Muhammad position for red dye
Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad in this context? Actually Muhammad differs for red) hold that the red-dyed cloth is not returned because the dye is a valuable increase (ziyada) that the thief owns; the owner's right is only in form, not in meaning. Muhammad (al-Shaybani) argues the cloth should be returned with compensation for the dye, treating it as usurpation (ghasb).
Note: 'Sahibayn' here refers to Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf? The commentary says 'وهو قولهما' referring to Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf? Actually the text uses 'قولهما' meaning the two (Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf?) but the dispute is with Muhammad.
4
Analyze the differing views on black dye
For black, Abu Hanifa and Muhammad say the thief must return the cloth because black is a defect (naqs) that does not sever the owner's right. Abu Yusuf treats black as an increase like red, so no return.
The commentator notes this difference is due to time and era, not clear proof.
5
Note the condition of dyeing before vs after cutting
The rulings apply to dyeing before cutting. If the thief dyes after cutting, the cloth must be returned (for red) because the partnership after cutting does not drop the hadd.
This is an important boundary condition.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (ولو صبغه أحمر فقطع لا يرد ولا يضمن ولو اسود يرده . بيان لثلاثة أحكام الأول وجوب القطع لأن قطع السارق باعتبار سرقة الثوب الأبيض وهو لم يملكه أبيض بوجه ما والمملوك للسارق إنما هو المصبوغ فصار كما إذا سرق حنطة فطحنها فإنه يقطع بالحنطة وإن ملك الدقيق الثاني عدم رده إلى المسروق منه وهو قولهما. وقال محمد : يؤخذ منه الثوب ويعطى ما زاد الصبغ فيه اعتباراً للغصب، والجامع كون الثوب أصلاً قائماً وكون الصبغ تابعاً ولهما أن الصبغ قائم صورة ومعنى حتى لو أراد مصبوغاً يضمن ما زاد الصبغ فيه، وحق المالك في الثوب قائم صورة لا معنى أنه غير مضمون على السارق بالهلاك وهو الحكم الثالث الذي أفاده بقوله «ولا يضمن» أي لا يرده حال قيامه ولا يضمنه حال استهلاكه بخلاف الغصب لأن حق كل واحد قائم صورة ومعنى فاستويا من هذا الوجه ،،، ورجحنا جانب المالك لما ذكرنا قيد بكونه صبغه قبل القطع بدليل فاء التعقيب لأنه لو صبغه بعد القطع يرده لأن الشركة بعد القطع لا تسقط القطع؛ كذا في شرح المختار . وذكر في الهداية الصبغ بعد القطع فإنه قال : وإن سرق ثوباً فقطع فصبغه أحمر لم يؤخذ منه الثوب ولا يضمن اهـ. وهو مفيد لأنه لو صبغه قبل القطع فالحكم كذلك بالأولى وكلام محمد دليل عليه أيضاً فإنه قال : سرق الثوب فقطع يده وقد صبغ الثوب أحمر لم يؤخذ منه الثوب قوله : ولو اسود يرد أي لو صبغة السارق أسود يرده على المالك أبي حنيفة ومحمد وعند أبي يوسف هذا والأول سواء لأن السواد عنده زيادة كالحمرة وعند محمد زيادة أيضاً كالحمرة لكنه لا يقطع حق المالك لما مر ، وعند أبي حنيفة السواد نقصان فلا يوجب انقطاع حق المالك. قالوا وهذا اختلاف عصر وزمان لا حجة وبرهان فإن الناس
His statement: (And if he dyes it red and is cut, he does not return nor guarantee, and if black, he returns it). Explanation of three rulings: first, obligation of cutting because the cutting of the thief is on account of stealing the white cloth, and he did not own it white in any way; what is owned by the thief is only the dyed cloth, so it is like when he stole wheat and ground it, he is cut for the wheat even though he owns the flour. Second, non-return to the owner is their (Sahibayn's) statement. Muhammad said: The cloth is taken from him and the increase due to the dye is given to him, considering it as usurpation; the common factor is that the cloth remains as a tangible original and the dye is subordinate. And for them (Sahibayn) that the dye is present in form and meaning, such that if he wanted dyed cloth, he would guarantee the increase due to the dye, and the owner's right in the cloth is present in form but not in meaning because it is not guaranteed on the thief in case of destruction. And this is the third ruling indicated by his statement 'does not guarantee' i.e., he does not return it while it exists nor guarantee it when destroyed, unlike usurpation because in usurpation each one's right is present in form and meaning, so they are equal in this respect... and we favored the owner's side for what we mentioned, conditioned that he dyes before cutting, as indicated by the particle of sequence, because if he dyes after cutting, he returns it because partnership after cutting does not drop the cutting; this is in Sharh al-Mukhtar. And in al-Hidayah, dyeing after cutting: he said, 'If he stole a cloth and was cut then dyed it red, the cloth is not taken from him nor does he guarantee. It is beneficial because if he dyed before cutting, the ruling is similarly even more so, and Muhammad's statement also indicates it, for he said: 'He stole the cloth and had his hand cut off while he had dyed the cloth red, the cloth is not taken from him.' His statement: 'and if black, he returns it' i.e., if the thief dyes it black, he returns it to the owner according to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad. According to Abu Yusuf, this and the first are the same because black for him is an increase like red. And according to Muhammad, it is also an increase like red, but it does not cut off the owner's right for the reason mentioned. And according to Abu Hanifa, black is a defect, so it does not cut off the owner's right. They said: This is a difference of period and time, not a proof or demonstration, for people...
Source pages p. 112

Commentary
This passage deals with the ruling when a thief dyes a stolen cloth red or black before being cut. The commentator explains three rulings: (1) Cutting is obligatory because the theft was of the white cloth, which the thief never owned; he only owns the dyed cloth as a new object. This is analogous to stealing wheat and grinding it. (2) The cloth is not returned to the owner according to Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), because the dye is a valuable addition that the thief owns, and the owner's right is only to compensation. Muhammad holds that the cloth is taken from the thief and he is given the increase from the dye, treating it as a case of usurpation where both have a share. (3) The thief does not guarantee the cloth if it is destroyed, according to Sahibayn, because the owner's right is only in form, not in meaning; in contrast, in usurpation, the owner's right is both in form and meaning. The commentator says they favored the owner's side, but only if the dyeing was before cutting; if after cutting, the cloth must be returned. For black dye, according to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad, the thief must return the black cloth to the owner because black is considered a defect (naqs) that does not sever the owner's right. Abu Yusuf treats black like red (an increase), so he holds the same ruling as red (no return). The commentator notes this is a difference of time and era, not a definitive proof.

Key Terms
صبغ (ṣabagh): Dyeing the stolen cloth; the type of dye (red vs black) affects the ruling on whether the cloth is returned.
زيادة (ziyādah): Increase; red dye is considered an increase that gives the thief ownership of the dyed cloth, while black is a defect that does not.
نقصان (nuqsān): Defect; black dye is considered a defect (according to Abu Hanifa) that does not sever the owner's right.
قطع (qaṭʿ): Cutting (amputation) as hadd penalty for theft.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the mufta bihi (relied-upon) Hanafi position, what is the ruling when a thief dyes a stolen cloth red before being cut?
A) The thief is cut, returns the cloth, and does not guarantee
B) The thief is cut, does not return the cloth, and does not guarantee
C) The thief is cut, returns the cloth, and guarantees the value
D) The thief is not cut because the cloth is now red

Key Takeaway
The color of the dye on a stolen cloth determines whether the thief keeps it (increase) or must return it (defect), with cutting remaining wajib in all cases.

Highway Robbery: Definition, Conditions, and the First Case
Opening of the chapter on highway robbery, defining conditions and the first of four cases.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi definition of qat' al-tariq, identify its conditions, and explain the punishment for the first case (intention only) based on the Hanafi interpretation of Quran 5:33.

Core Idea
Mere intention to rob with intimidation is enough for hadd imprisonment; exile means imprisonment.

This lesson opens the chapter on highway robbery, a severe offense distinct from ordinary theft. The Hanafi school derives its rulings from Quran 5:33, interpreting 'exile' as imprisonment. We will explore the conditions that turn an armed robber into a 'qati' al-tariq' and the first of four scenarios: the robber who intends but has not yet killed or stolen.

Guided Path
1
Define qat' al-tariq
What is highway robbery in Hanafi fiqh? Identify the distinguishing elements from ordinary theft: use of force, outside settled areas, travel distance.
It is al-sariqa al-kubra (the greater theft).
2
Identify the conditions
List the three conditions for the hadd punishment: possession of power (shawkah), commitment outside a city (misr), and travel distance between towns. Note Abu Yusuf's view allowing inside a city by night.
These are from zaahir al-riwaayah.
3
Analyze the Quranic proof
Quran 5:33: 'or be exiled from the land'. Hanafis interpret 'nafy' as imprisonment because it removes the person from normal society. This proof applies to the first case.
The dalalah is zanni because of the linguistic interpretation.
4
Apply to the first case
The robber is caught after intending but before killing or stealing. Ruling: imprisonment until repentance. Tazir may also be added per al-Hidayah.
The pronoun 'qablahu' refers to cutting the road, not to an unmentioned act.
5
Understand the disagreement
A commentator claimed mere intimidation is 'cutting', but the author rejects this. In Hanafi fiqh, intimidation alone constitutes the first case, not the full act of highway robbery.
This affects only categorisation, not the punishment.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب قطع الطريق أخذ قاصد قطع الطريق قبله حبس حتى يتوب وإن أخذ مالاً معصوماً قطع يده من خلاف وإن قتل حدا وإن عفا الولي وإن قتل وأخذ قطع وقتل أو صلب أو قتل وصلب بيان لأحوال قاطع الطريق فبين أنها أربع الأولى لو أمسك بعد ما قصد قطع الطريق ولم يقطعها على أحد وحكمه الحبس حتى يتوب وهو المراد بقوله تعالى أو ينفوا من الأرض [المائدة : ٣٣] فالنفي بمعنى الحبس لأنه نفي عن وجه الأرض وقد عهد عقوبة في الشرع، ولم يذكر المصنف التعزير وفي الهداية : ويعزرون أيضاً لمباشرتهم منكر الإخافة اهـ. أخذه وأطلق في فشمل ما إذا كان بإذن الإمام أو لا، ولم يبينوا بماذا يتحقق قصده لظهور أنه يحصل بوقوفه على الطريق لإخافة المارين، وأما قطع الطريق حقيقة فبالقتل أو أخذ المال وأن يكون بالإخافة فقط، فالضمير في قوله «قبله عائد إلى قطع الطريق لا كما قال الشارح أنها ترجع إلى غير مذكور، وكلامه مبني على أن مجرد الإخافة قطع وليس كذلك والتوبة وإن
Chapter: Highway Robbery (Qat' al-Tariq) Taking the one who intends to cut the road before it: imprisonment until he repents. And if he takes protected wealth: amputation of his hand from the opposite side? And if he kills: hadd, even if the guardian pardons. And if he kills and takes: amputation and killing, or crucifixion, or killing and crucifixion. Explanation of the states of the highway robber: he explains that they are four: The first: if he is seized after he intended to cut the road but did not carry it out against anyone, his ruling is imprisonment until he repents, and that is intended by His statement: 'or be exiled from the land' [5:33]. So exile means imprisonment because it is removal from the face of the earth and it is a known punishment in the Law. The author did not mention tazir, but in al-Hidaya: 'And they are also punished (tazir) for committing the evil of intimidation' etc. He took and generalized? So it includes whether (the arrest) is by the Imam's permission or not. They did not explain by what his intention is achieved, since it is clear that it occurs by his standing on the road to intimidate passersby. As for cutting the road in reality, it is by killing or taking wealth, and that it is only by intimidation. So the pronoun in 'before it' refers to cutting the road, not as the commentator said that it refers to something not mentioned. His statement is based on the idea that mere intimidation is cutting, and it is not so. And repentance, even though...
Source pages p. 113

Commentary
This passage introduces the chapter on highway robbery (qat' al-tariq), which is considered an aggravated form of theft (al-sariqa al-kubra). The author lists the four situations of the highway robber. The first situation is when the robber intends to rob but has not yet harmed anyone (no killing or theft). The punishment is imprisonment until he repents. This is derived from the Quranic verse 5:33, where 'exile' (nafy) is interpreted as imprisonment, as it removes the person from normal society. The text clarifies that mere intention or intimidation is sufficient for this case. The passage also notes a difference of opinion: whether 'cutting the road' is achieved by mere intimidation or requires actual killing or theft. The author rejects the view that intimidation alone constitutes the full act. Additionally, the commentary from al-Hidaya adds that a tazir (discretionary punishment) may also be applied. The conditions for highway robbery are mentioned implicitly: the robber must have power and strength, not be within a settled city or equivalent, and a travel distance must exist between towns. The four cases are: (1) intention only → imprisonment; (2) takes wealth but does not kill → amputation of hand and foot from opposite sides; (3) kills but does not take wealth → qisas (retaliation), even if the guardian forgives (the hadd remains); (4) kills and takes wealth → options of qisas and crucifixion, or qisas alone, or crucifixion alone.

Key Terms
قطع الطريق (qat' al-tariq): Highway robbery or banditry; an aggravated theft committed by armed individuals outside settled areas.
نفي (nafy): Exile; interpreted by Hanafis as imprisonment, as removal from normal society.
إخافة (ikhafah): Intimidation of travelers, which constitutes the act of highway robbery even if no killing or theft occurs.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) Physical banishment to another land.
B) Imprisonment.
C) Execution.
D) Flogging.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, the first case of highway robbery (intention only) is punished by imprisonment based on Quran 5:33's 'exile' interpreted as removal from society.

The Four Cases of Highway Robbery and Their Punishments
Explaining the four cases of highway robbers with their punishments and conditions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the four cases of highway robbery according to the Hanafi school, identify the punishment for each case, and explain the intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the fourth case.

Core Idea
The punishment for highway robbery is graded according to the severity of the crime, with the Imam having discretion in the most severe case.

This lesson unpacks the Hanafi ruling on qat' al-tariq, deriving four distinct scenarios from Quran 5:33. The text explains the conditions for each punishment and discusses an intra-Hanafi disagreement on whether amputation can be added in the fourth case. Understanding these distinctions is essential for correctly applying the hadd penalty.

Guided Path
1
Identify the four cases
From the passage, list the four scenarios based on what the robber has done: mere intention, taking property without killing, killing without taking property, and both killing and taking property.
The fourth case is the most severe and gives the Imam a choice.
2
Understand the conditions for each punishment
For the second case, note the two conditions: the property must be protected (belongs to Muslim or dhimmi) and reach nisab. For the third case, note that it is a hadd, not qisas, so pardon is irrelevant.
The nisab condition is the same as in theft (sariqa).
3
Examine the Quranic evidence (dalil)
Read Quran 5:33 and see how the Hanafi school distributes the four punishments among the four cases. The verse lists banishment, cutting, killing, and crucifixion, each assigned to a specific scenario.
The imperative form indicates obligation, but the distribution is based on the context.
4
Analyze the intra-Hanafi dispute on the fourth case
Imam Muhammad prohibits cutting in addition to killing/crucifixion, arguing it is a single crime not deserving two hadds. Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf allow it as a single aggravated hadd. Determine which position is mufta bihi.
The relied-upon view (zahir al-riwaya) gives the Imam the choice to include cutting or not.
5
Determine the mufta bihi position
The text states the apparent transmission is that the Imam has a choice between combining all three punishments, or just killing, or just crucifixion. Cutting is not mandatory.
Al-Marghinani in al-Hidayah supports this view.

باب قطع الطريق
أخذ قاصد قطع الطريق قبله حبس حتى يتوب وإن أخذ مالاً معصوماً قطع يده ورجله من خلاف وإن قتل حدا وإن عفا الولي وإن قتل وأخذ قطع وقتل أو صلب أو قتل وصلب) بيان لأحوال قاطع الطريق فبين أنها أربع الأولى لو
