والتوبة وإن كانت متعلقة بالقلب لكن لحصولها أمارات ظاهرة فصح أن تكون غاية للحبس. الثانية أن يؤخذ بعد ما أخذ المال ولم يقتل النفس، وحكمه أن تقطع يده اليمنى ورجله اليسرى بشرطين: أحدهما أن يكون ذلك المال معصوماً وهو أن يكون المسلم أو ذمي فخرج مال المستأمن. الثاني أن يكون نصاباً ولم يصرح به للاكتفاء بذكره في السرقة الصغرى فلا يقطع على من أصابه أقل من نصاب وهو المراد بقوله تعالى أو تقطع أيديهم وأرجلهم من خلاف [المائدة : ٣٣] بناء على أن الأجزية متوزعة على الأحوال كما علم في الأصول، ولما كانت جنايته أفحش من السرقة الصغرى كانت عقوبته أغلظ ، وإنما كان من خلاف لئلا تفوت جنس المنفعه ولذا لو كانت يده اليسرى مقطوعة أو شلاء أو رجله اليمني كذلك لا يقطع الثالثة أن يؤخذ بعد ما قتل نفساً معصومة ولم يأخذ مالاً، وحكمه أن الإمام يقتله حداً الله تعالى لا قصاصاً حتى لو عفا الأولياء لا يلتفت إلى عفوهم. وأشار بكونه حداً إلى أنه لا يشترط في القتل أن يكون موجباً للقصاص من مباشرة الكل والآلة لأنه وجب في مقابلة الحناية على حق الله تعالى بمحاربته ولذا قال في المجتبى : ويقتل الكل في الحالة الثالثة حداً القاتل والمعين فيه سواء. وإنما الشرط القتل من أحدهم، وسواء قتلهم بسيف أو حجر أو عصا أو غيرهما، ويصير كالجماعة قتلوا واحداً به قضى رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم في أصحاب أبي بردة اهـ. الرابعة أن يؤخذ وقد قتل النفس وأخذ المال فذكر المصنف أن الإمام مخير بين أشياء : إما أن يجمع بين الثلاثة قطع اليد والرجل من خلاف والقتل والصلب، وإما أن يقتصر على القتل وإما أن يقتصر على الصلب وهكذا في الهداية. ومنع محمد القطع لأنه جناية واحدة فلا توجب حدين ولأن ما دون النفس يدخل في النفس في باب الحد كحد السرقة والرجم ولهما أن هذه عقوبة واحدة تغلظت لتغلظ سببها وهو تفويت الأمن على التناهي بالقتل وأخذ المال ولهذا كان قطع اليد والرجل معاً في الكبرى حداً واحداً وإن كان في الصغرى حدين، والتداخل في الحدود لا في حد واحد، ثم ذكر في الكتاب التخيير بين الصلب وتركه وهو ظاهر الرواية . وعن أبي يوسف أنه لا يتركه لأنه منصوص عليه والمقصود التشهير ليعتبر به غيره ونحن نقول : أصل التشهير بالقتل والمبالغة بالصلب فيخير فيه .
Book of Theft, Chapter of Highway Robbery. Chapter of Highway Robbery: 'One who intends highway robbery before it is taken [i.e., before he commits it] is imprisoned until he repents; if he takes protected property, his hand is cut; if he kills, [he is killed] as a fixed penalty even if the guardian pardons; if he kills and takes [property], he is [subject to] cutting, killing, or crucifixion, or killing and crucifixion.' Explanation of the conditions of the highway robber: [The commentator] explains that they are four. First: if he is seized after intending highway robbery but has not committed it against anyone, his ruling is imprisonment until he repents, and this is the meaning of the Exalted's statement 'Or they be banished from the land' [5:33]. So 'banishment' means imprisonment because it is banishment from the face of the earth, and it is a prescribed punishment in the law. The author did not mention ta'zir, but in al-Hidayah: 'They are also given discretionary punishment for directly committing the evil of intimidation.' End. His statement 'he is seized' is general, including whether it is with the Imam's permission or not, and they did not explain how his intent is realized, as it is apparent that it occurs by his standing on the road to intimidate passersby. As for actual highway robbery, it is by killing or taking property, and that it be solely by intimidation. So the pronoun in his statement 'before it' refers to 'highway robbery', not as the commentator said that it refers to something unmentioned. His statement is based on the idea that mere intimidation is cutting, which is not the case. Repentance, even if it is related to the heart, has apparent signs, so it is valid as a limit for imprisonment. Second: if he is seized after taking property but not killing a person, his ruling is that his right hand and left foot are cut, under two conditions: one, that the property is protected, meaning it belongs to a Muslim or a dhimmi, so the property of a musta'min is excluded. Two, that it reaches the nisab, and he did not explicitly mention it because mentioning it in the chapter on minor theft suffices, so it is not cut for one who takes less than the nisab. This is the meaning of the Exalted's statement 'Or their hands and feet be cut off from opposite sides' [5:33], based on the principle that the penalties are distributed according to the states as known in usul. Since his crime is more heinous than minor theft, his punishment is more severe. It is from opposite sides so that the benefit of the limb is not lost entirely; thus if his left hand is already cut or paralyzed, or his right foot similarly, he is not cut. Third: if he is seized after killing a protected soul but not taking property, his ruling is that the Imam kills him as a fixed penalty (hadd) of Allah, not as qisas, so even if the guardians pardon, their pardon is not considered. His indication that it is a hadd indicates that the killing does not require the conditions of qisas such as direct participation and use of a specific tool, because it is imposed in response to aggression against the right of Allah by waging war. Therefore, it is said in al-Mujtaba: 'All are killed in the third state as a hadd, the killer and the aider are the same in it.' The only condition is the killing by one of them, whether they kill with a sword, stone, stick, or other, and it is like a group who killed one person, as the Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) ruled in the case of the companions of Abu Barda. End. Fourth: if he is seized after having killed a person and taken property, the author states that the Imam has a choice among things: either he combines the three: cutting the hand and foot from opposite sides, killing, and crucifixion; or he limits it to killing; or he limits it to crucifixion. This is in al-Hidayah. Muhammad prohibited the additional cutting because it is a single offense that does not necessitate two hadds, and because what is less than life is included in life in the chapter of hadd like the hadd of theft and stoning. But Abu Yusuf and [Abu Hanifa] argue that this is a single punishment that is intensified due to the intensification of its cause, which is the destruction of security to the utmost through killing and taking property. Therefore, cutting the hand and foot together in the major [highway robbery] is a single hadd, even though in minor [theft] it is two hadds, and the rule of merging applies to multiple hadds, not within a single hadd. Then al-Quduri mentioned the choice between crucifixion and leaving it, which is the apparent transmission. From Abu Yusuf, he does not leave it because it is textually prescribed and the purpose is public display for others to take heed. But we say: the origin of public display is through killing, and exaggeration is through crucifixion, so he has a choice.
Source pages p. 113

Commentary
This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq explains the rulings for highway robbers (qutta' al-tariq). It begins by listing four distinct cases based on what the robber has done, each with a specific punishment derived from Quran 5:33. The first case is for one who intends robbery but has not yet carried it out; he is imprisoned until he repents. The second case is for one who takes protected property but does not kill; his right hand and left foot are cut off. The third case is for one who kills but does not take property; he is executed as a hadd penalty, not as qisas, so the victim's family's pardon is irrelevant. The fourth case is for one who both kills and takes property; here the imam has a choice between combining amputation, execution, and crucifixion, or just execution, or just crucifixion. The passage also discusses an intra-Hanafi dispute: Imam Muhammad argues that in the fourth case the additional amputation should not be applied because it is a single crime, while Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf consider it a single aggravated hadd. The relied-upon view (mufta bihi) is the apparent narration (zahir al-riwaya) that the imam may choose. The conditions for the punishment include that the property taken reaches nisab and is protected (i.e., from a Muslim or dhimmi).

Key Terms
قطع الطريق (qat' al-tariq): Highway robbery, defined as armed robbery or banditry that threatens travelers, encompassing intimidation, theft, and murder.
حد (hadd): A fixed punishment prescribed by Allah, not subject to pardon or reduction.
صلب (salb): Crucifixion, a punishment where the body is displayed after execution as a deterrent.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school classifies highway robbery into four cases with graded punishments, and in the most severe case the Imam has discretion to include or exclude amputation.

Highway Robbery Without Killing: Punishment and Conditions
States the ruling for the second case of highway robbery with conditions and Quranic proof.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling for a highway robber who takes property without killing, identify the two conditions required for amputation, and explain the rationale for cutting opposite limbs using the Quranic proof.

Core Idea
For highway robbery without killing, the Hanafi school mandates cutting the right hand and left foot — a punishment stricter than minor theft, derived from Quran 5:33.

Highway robbery (qat' al-tariq) is a heinous crime that disrupts public safety. The Hanafi school distinguishes this case from simple theft by imposing a more severe punishment: amputation of the right hand and left foot. This lesson explains the ruling, its conditions, and its scriptural basis, ensuring you understand how the school derives this unique punishment from the Quran and applies it in practice.

Guided Path
1
Identify the crime and its category
Recognize that the source passage addresses the second category of highway robber: one who takes property but does not kill. The punishment is amputation of the right hand and left foot from opposite sides.
This is distinct from simple theft (sariqa) where only the right hand is cut.
2
List the two conditions for amputation
The passage states two conditions: (1) the property must be ma'sum (protected), meaning it belongs to a Muslim or dhimmi, and (2) the property must reach the nisab threshold (same as minor theft). The property of musta'min and harbi is excluded.
Nisab in Hanafi fiqh is 10 dirhams or equivalent value.
3
Cite the Quranic proof and explain its application
The verse 'or their hands and feet be cut off from opposite sides' (Quran 5:33) is the primary dalil. The distribution of punishments according to states is a usul principle: since highway robbery is more severe than minor theft, the punishment is more severe — cutting opposite limbs.
The cutting is from opposite sides to preserve some function.
4
Note the exception for missing or paralyzed limbs
If the left hand or right foot is already missing or paralyzed, the hadd cannot be applied because the punishment would not achieve its intended effect.
This shows the punishment is designed to be precise and not overly harsh.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثانية أن يؤخذ بعد ما أخذ المال ولم يقتل النفس، وحكمه أن تقطع يده اليمنى ورجله اليسرى بشرطين: أحدهما أن يكون ذلك المال معصوماً وهو أن يكون المسلم أو ذمي فخرج مال المستأمن والحربي. الثاني أن يكون نصاباً ولم يصرح به للاكتفاء بذكره في السرقة الصغرى فلا قطع على من أصابه أقل من نصاب وهو المراد بقوله تعالى أو تقطع أيديهم وأرجلهم من خلاف [المائدة : ٣٣] بناء على أن الأجزية متوزعة على الأحوال كما علم في الأصول، ولما كانت جنايته أفحش من السرقة الصغرى كانت عقوبته أغلظ ، وإنما كان من خلاف لئلا تفوت جنس المنفعه ولذا لو كانت يده اليسرى مقطوعة أو شلاء أو رجله اليمني كذلك لا يقطع.
The second: that he is seized after he took property and did not kill a soul, and its ruling is that his right hand and left foot are cut off with two conditions: one of them is that that wealth be protected, which is that it be a Muslim or a dhimmi, so the wealth of a musta'min and a harbi is excluded. The second is that it be a nisab, and he did not explicitly state it, relying on its mention in the minor theft, so there is no cutting for one who is struck with less than nisab, and that is what is meant by His statement: 'or their hands and feet be cut off from opposite sides' [al-Ma'idah 5:33], based on the fact that the punishments are distributed according to the states as known in usul, and since his crime is more heinous than minor theft, his punishment is more severe, and it is from opposite sides so that the benefit of the limb is not lost entirely, and so if his left hand is already cut off or paralyzed or his right foot similarly, he is not cut.

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof, detailing the punishment for the second category of highway robber: one who takes property but does not kill. The author states that the punishment is amputation of the right hand and left foot from opposite sides, with two conditions. First, the property taken must be 'protected' (ma'sum), meaning it belongs to a Muslim or a dhimmi. The property of a musta'min (temporary safe-conduct holder) or a harbi (enemy combatant) is not protected, so the hadd does not apply. Second, the property must reach the nisab threshold, which is the same as for minor theft. The proof is the Quranic verse 'or their hands and feet be cut off from opposite sides,' which the commentator explains applies to this case because the punishments are distributed according to the severity of the crimes. The cutting is from opposite sides to avoid total loss of function of both limbs. If the left hand or right foot is already missing or paralyzed, the punishment cannot be carried out.

Key Terms
معصوماً (ma'suman): Protected property that cannot lawfully be taken; applies to Muslims and dhimmis.
نصاب (nisab): The minimum value of property for which the hadd of theft applies; same as in minor theft.
من خلاف (min khilaf): Cutting from opposite sides (right hand and left foot) to avoid total loss of function.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway

Ruling on Highway Robber Who Kills Without Taking Property
States the ruling for the third case of highway robbery: killing only, and its punishment as hadd.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling for highway robbery where the robber kills but does not take property, and explain why the hadd punishment is not affected by the victim's heirs' pardon.

Core Idea
Killing alone in highway robbery triggers a hadd execution for violating Allah's right—heirs' pardon is irrelevant.

This lesson examines the third category of highway robbery (hirabah): the robber who kills a protected person but takes no property. The Hanafi school holds that the imam must execute such a robber as a hadd punishment, distinct from qisas, meaning the punishment cannot be waived by the victim's family. We will explore the reasoning behind this ruling, based on the text from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq.

Guided Path
1
Identify the case
Recognize that this is the third scenario of highway robbery: the robber kills a protected soul (Muslim or dhimmi) but does not steal property.
The key distinction: killing without theft.
2
State the ruling
The imam must execute the robber as a hadd punishment for Allah, not as qisas for the victim.
Hadd is a fixed divine punishment; qisas is a private right that can be pardoned.
3
Understand why it is hadd, not qisas
Because the offense is against Allah's right (waging war against Him), the punishment is a hadd. Therefore, conditions for qisas (direct participation, weapon) are not required, and the heirs' pardon is irrelevant.
The text emphasizes that the killing is as a hadd, so it does not depend on qisas eligibility.
4
Recognize the scope
All participants—the killer and the aider—are equally liable for execution as a hadd, as long as at least one of them caused the death. The method of killing (sword, stone, etc.) is irrelevant.
This follows the precedent of the Prophet's judgment regarding the companions of Abu Barza.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثالثة أن يؤخذ بعد ما قتل نفساً معصومة ولم يأخذ مالاً، وحكمه أن الإمام يقتله حداً لله تعالى لا قصاصاً حتى لو عفا الأولياء لا يلتفت إلى عفوهم. وأشار بكونه حداً إلى أنه لا يشترط في القتل أن يكون موجباً للقصاص من مباشرة الكل والآلة لأنه وجب في مقابلة الجناية على حق الله تعالى بمحاربته ولذا قال في المجتبى: ويقتل الكل في الحالة الثالثة حداً القاتل والمعين فيه سواء. وإنما الشرط القتل من أحدهم، وسواء قتلهم بسيف أو حجر أو عصا أو غيرهما، ويصير كالجماعة قتلوا واحداً به قضى رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم في أصحاب أبي بردة اهـ.
The third: that he is seized after he killed a protected soul and did not take property, and its ruling is that the imam kills him as a hadd for Allah, not as qisas, so even if the heirs pardon, no attention is paid to their pardon. And he indicated by its being a hadd that it is not required for the killing to be subject to qisas in terms of direct participation and instrument, because it became obligatory in exchange for the offense against the right of Allah Almighty by waging war against Him. For this reason, it is said in al-Mujtaba: 'All are killed in the third case as a hadd, the killer and the aider being equal.' The condition is only the killing by one of them, and it is the same whether they kill with a sword, a stone, a stick, or other than them, and it becomes like a group that killed one person; the Messenger of Allah, may Allah bless him and grant him peace, judged accordingly in the case of the companions of Abu Barza.
Source pages p. 114

Commentary
This passage is a ruling statement for the third category of highway robber: one who kills but does not take property. The punishment is execution by the imam as a hadd penalty (for violating Allah's right), not as qisas (retaliation). This means that even if the victim's heirs forgive, the punishment is not waived. The text then elaborates that because it is a hadd, the conditions for qisas—such as direct participation and use of a weapon—are not required. All participants are killed, as stated in al-Mujtaba, whether they were the actual killer or an aider. The only condition is that at least one of them caused the death. The method of killing is irrelevant; it includes any instrument. The precedent of the Prophet's judgment regarding the companions of Abu Barza is cited, where a group was held liable for killing one person.

Key Terms
حداً (haddan): A fixed punishment prescribed by Allah, here execution for highway robbery involving killing.
نفساً معصومة (nafsan ma'sumah): A protected soul, i.e., a life that cannot be lawfully taken; includes Muslims and dhimmis.
المعين (al-mu'in): The aider or accomplice in the killing, who is also subject to the hadd punishment.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why is the pardon of the victim's heirs irrelevant in the third case of highway robbery?
A) Because the punishment is qisas, which cannot be pardoned.
B) Because the punishment is a hadd for Allah's right, not a private right.
C) Because the heirs are not present at the time of the crime.
D) Because the imam has absolute authority to execute regardless.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, a highway robber who kills but does not steal is executed as a hadd for violating Allah's right; the pardon of the victim's heirs has no effect.

The Punishment for a Highway Robber Who Kills and Takes Property (Intra-Hanafi Dispute)
States the ruling for the fourth case of highway robbery and the intra-Hanafi dispute regarding punishment.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on the punishment for a highway robber who kills and takes property, identify the intra-Hanafi dispute regarding amputation, and explain the reasoning of each side.

Core Idea
A single severe crime earns a single intensified punishment—not two separate hadds.

This lesson examines the fourth scenario of highway robbery (hirabah): a robber who both kills and takes property. The Hanafi school gives the imam a choice among three punitive options, but there is a notable dispute within the school—Imam Muhammad al-Shaybani objects to including amputation, while Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf consider the entire punishment as one intensified hadd. Understanding this dispute sharpens your ability to apply the principle of tadakhul (overlapping) and appreciate the school's internal reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the fourth case and the three options
Recognize that the robber has committed two elements: killing and theft. The imam may choose: (1) amputation (right hand, left foot) plus execution and crucifixion; (2) execution alone; or (3) crucifixion alone.
The options are mutually exclusive; the imam selects one.
2
Understand Imam Muhammad's objection to amputation
Imam Muhammad argues that since there is only one crime (highway robbery), it cannot incur two separate hadd punishments. He compares it to theft and stoning where the minor hadd is subsumed.
His proof is based on the principle that a single act does not generate multiple hadds.
3
Analyze the response of Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf
They respond that this is a single punishment intensified due to the severe cause (loss of security and life). Amputation of hand and foot together is considered one hadd in this context, not two separate ones.
The key distinction: overlapping (tadakhul) applies between different hadds, not within a single compounded hadd.
4
Note the disagreement on crucifixion
Abu Yusuf insists on crucifixion because it is textually prescribed, while the apparent transmission (zahir al-riwayah) allows the imam to choose. The author reconciles by stating that the purpose of public exposure is achieved by execution, and crucifixion is an enhancement.
This shows the range of ikhtilaf within the school even on the method.
5
Determine the mufta bihi and its rationale
The preferred view (mufta bihi) is crucifixion alive for three days, as it is the apparent madhhab and most correct. The three-day limit avoids harm to the public, and after death the body is released for burial.
Al-Tahawi's opinion (execute first then crucify) is considered weaker.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الرابعة أن يؤخذ وقد قتل النفس وأخذ المال فذكر المصنف أن الإمام مخير بين أشياء : إما أن يجمع بين الثلاثة قطع اليد والرجل من خلاف والقتل والصلب، وإما أن يقتصر على القتل وإما أن يقتصر على الصلب وهكذا في الهداية. ومنع محمد القطع لأنه جناية واحدة فلا توجب حدين ولأن ما دون النفس يدخل في النفس في باب الحد كحد السرقة والرجم ولهما أن هذه عقوبة واحدة تغلظت لتغلظ سببها وهو تفويت الأمن على التناهي بالقتل وأخذ المال ولهذا كان قطع اليد والرجل معاً في الكبرى حداً واحداً وإن كان في الصغرى حدين، والتداخل في الحدود لا في حد واحد، ثم ذكر في الكتاب التخيير بين الصلب وتركه وهو ظاهر الرواية . وعن أبي يوسف أنه لا يتركه لأنه منصوص عليه والمقصود التشهير ليعتبر به غيره ونحن نقول : أصل التشهير بالقتل والمبالغة بالصلب فيخير فيه . قوله : (ويصلب حياً ثلاثة أيام ويبعج بطنه برمح حتى يموت) تشهيراً له واستعجالاً لموته، ومعنى يبعج يشق ؛ كذا في المغرب. والصلب حياً ظاهر المذهب كما في المجتبى وهو الأصح. وعند الطحاوي أنه يقتل ثم يصلب. وقيد بالثلاثة لأنه لا يصلب أكثر منها توقياً عن تأذي الناس فإذا ثم له ثلاثة من وقت موته يخلي بينه وبين أهله ليدفنوه. وعن أبي يوسف أنه يترك على الخشبة حتى يتقطع فيسقط.
The fourth: that he is seized after he killed the soul and took property. The author mentioned that the imam has a choice among things: either to combine all three: cutting the hand and foot from opposite sides, killing, and crucifixion; or to restrict to killing; or to restrict to crucifixion; and so on in al-Hidayah. Muhammad prohibited the cutting because it is a single crime so it does not necessitate two hadds, and because what is less than the soul is subsumed under the soul in the chapter of hadd, like the hadd of theft and stoning. For them (Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf), this is a single punishment that is made severe because its cause is severe: the destruction of security to the utmost by killing and taking property. For this reason, cutting the hand and foot together in the greater [theft] is one hadd, although it is two hadds in the minor [theft]. And overlapping is in hadds, not in one hadd. Then he mentioned in the book the choice between crucifixion and leaving it, and that is the apparent transmission. And from Abu Yusuf that he does not leave it because it is textually prescribed and the purpose is public exposure so that others may take heed. And we say: The basis of public exposure is killing, and exaggeration is by crucifixion, so there is choice in it. His saying: (And he is crucified alive for three days and his abdomen is slit with a spear until he dies) for his public exposure and hastening his death. The meaning of yub'aju is to slit; so in al-Mughrib. Crucifixion alive is the apparent madhhab as in al-Mujtaba, and it is the most correct. According to al-Tahawi, he is killed then crucified. It is restricted to three because he is not crucified more than that to avoid harming people. Then after three days from his death, he is handed over to his family to bury him. From Abu Yusuf, that he is left on the wood until he falls apart.
Source pages p. 114

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the punishment for a highway robber who both kills and takes property. The author states that the imam has a choice among three options: combine amputation (right hand and left foot), execution, and crucifixion; or restrict to execution; or restrict to crucifixion. Imam Muhammad al-Shaybani opposes the inclusion of amputation on two grounds: (1) it is a single crime (the highway robbery) which should not incur two separate hadd punishments, and (2) minor acts (like theft of property) are subsumed under the major act (killing) just as in the hadd of theft and stoning (where lashes are subsumed under stoning). In response, Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf argue that this is actually a single punishment that is intensified due to the severity of the cause—the destruction of security through both killing and taking property. Therefore, the amputation of hand and foot together in this major case is considered one hadd, even though in minor theft they are two separate hadds (only hand cut). The overlapping principle applies to multiple hadds, not within a single compounded hadd. Then the text discusses the choice regarding crucifixion: the apparent transmission allows leaving it out, but Abu Yusuf insists on it because it is textually mandated for public deterrent. The author reconciles by stating that public exposure is achieved by execution, and crucifixion is an enhancement, hence there is choice. The passage then details the method of crucifixion: the robber is crucified alive for three days, his abdomen slit with a spear to hasten death. This is the preferred view (as in al-Mujtaba). Al-Tahawi holds he is killed first then crucified. The three-day limit is to avoid harm to people. After three days from death, the body is released for burial. Abu Yusuf held that the body should be left until it decays and falls.

Key Terms
مخير (mukhayyar): The imam has a choice among three options for punishment in the fourth case.
صلب (salb): Crucifixion, a punishment involving fixing the body on a cross; here done alive.
تداخل (tadakhul): Overlapping of punishments; if multiple hadds are due, they may merge.
بعج (ba'aja): To slit or rip open the abdomen with a spear.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is Imam Muhammad's objection to including amputation in the punishment for a highway robber who kills and takes property?
A) He believes amputation is too lenient.
B) He argues that one crime cannot incur two separate hadds.
C) He thinks the imam should have no choice.
D) He prefers only execution.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school treats the punishment for a highway robber who kills and takes property as a single intensified hadd, allowing the imam to choose among three options, with the preferred view being crucifixion alive for three days.

Additional Rulings on Highway Robbery: Non-Direct Participation, Weapons, and Exceptions to Hadd
States rulings on non-direct participants, weapons, and six exceptions to hadd.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the six exceptions where the hadd of highway robbery (hirabah) is not applied, and explain the Hanafi rulings on accomplices and the definition of a weapon.

Core Idea
Accomplices and any weapon are treated identically in highway robbery, but six exceptions drop the hadd and relegate the case to private claims.

The previous lesson established the basic hadd for highway robbery. This passage expands on three critical dimensions: how the ruling applies to non-direct participants, what qualifies as a weapon, and the exceptional cases where the hadd is not imposed. These exceptions reveal the Hanafi balancing act between upholding divine limits (hudud Allah) and preserving individual rights (huquq al-ibad) in specific circumstances.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling on non-direct participants
The text states that non-direct participants (ghayr al-mubashir) are treated like the direct participant in taking and killing. All share the ruling even if only one commits the act, because the crime is waging war (muharaba).
This differs from qisas, where only the direct killer may be liable.
2
Determine what qualifies as a weapon
The text equates a stick and stone with a sword. The criterion is cutting off the road by harming passersby. Any instrument that can effect the robbery qualifies.
Thus, the type of weapon does not matter; only the capacity to obstruct the highway matters.
3
Understand the effect of hadd on civil liability
When hadd is enforced, the robber is not liable for the property taken or for wounds inflicted; the divine right (hadd) supersedes individual claims. However, in the exceptions, the hadd is dropped and private claims (qisas or diya) come into play.
This is analogous to minor theft (sariqa sughra) where after hadd, the property is not returned.
4
List the six exceptions where hadd is not applied
Identify each case from the text: (1) wounding without killing or taking property; (2) killing only and then repenting; (3) some robbers are non-mukallaf (insane/minor); (4) robber is a mahram relative of the victim; (5) part of a caravan preys on another part; (6) robbery occurs within a city (misr).
In all these cases, the case becomes a private matter of qisas or blood money.
5
Analyze the critical note on the first exception
The commentator notes a consideration (nazar): the right belongs to the wounded, not his guardian; if the wound leads to death, hadd should be obligatory. This shows that the exceptions are subject to further scrutiny.
This intra-Hanafi refinement demonstrates the dynamic nature of fiqh.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (ولم يضمن ما أخذ) يعني بعد ما أقيم عليه الحد كما في السرقة الصغرى ولو قال ولم يضمن ما فعل لكان أولى لأنه لا يضمن ما قتل وما جرح لذلك المعنى قوله : (غير المباشر كالمباشر يعني في الأخذ والقتل حتى تجري الأحكام على الكل بمباشرة البعض لأنه جزاء المحاربة وهي تتحقق بأن يكون البعض رداً للبعض حتى إذا زالت أقدامهم انحازوا اليهم وإنما الشرط القتل من واحد منهم وقد تحقق قوله: (والعصا والحجر (كالسيف لأنه يقع قطعاً للطريق بقطع المارة قوله : (وإن أخذ مالاً وجرح قطع وبطل الجرح بيان للحالة الخامسة لهم وهي أن يأخذ المال ويجرح إنساناً فيقطع يده ورجله من خلاف ولا يجب شيء لأجل الجرح لأنه لما الحد حقاً الله تعالى سقطت وجب عصمة النفس حقاً للعبد كما تسقط عصمة المال . قوله : (وإن جرح فقط أو قتل فتاب أو كان بعض القطاع غير مكلف أو ذا رحم محرم من المقطوع عليه أو قطع بعض القافلة على البعض أو قطع الطريق ليلاً أو نهاراً بمصر مصرين لم يحد فأقاد الولي أو عفا بيان للمسائل التي لا حد فيها وهي ست مسائل : الأولى لو جرح ولم يقتل ولم يأخذ مالاً فلأنه لا حد في هذه الجناية فيظهر حق العبد فيقتص منه مما فيه القصاص وأخذ الأرش منه مما فيه الأرش وذلك إلى الأولياء؛ كذا في الهداية. وفيه نظر لأن ذلك للمجروح لا لوليه فإن أفضى الجرح إلى القتل ينبغي أن يجب الحد، ولما كان أخذ المال
His saying: (And he is not liable for what he took) means after the hadd is established upon him as in minor theft, and if he said 'and he is not liable for what he did' it would be better, because he is not liable for what he killed or wounded for that meaning. His saying: (The non-direct participant is like the direct participant) means in taking and killing, so that the rulings apply to all by the direct action of some, because it is the recompense for waging war, and it is realized by some being a deterrent to others until when their feet fail they retreat to them. The condition is only the killing by one of them, and it is realized. His saying: (The stick and stone are like the sword) because cutting the road occurs by cutting off passersby. His saying: (And if he took property and wounded, he is cut and the wound becomes void) is an explanation of the fifth case for them, which is that he takes property and wounds a person, so his hand and foot are cut from opposite sides, and nothing is owed for the wound, because when the hadd is established as a right of Allah, the protection of the soul falls as a right of the slave, just as the protection of property falls. His saying: (And if he wounded only or killed and then repented, or some of the highway robbers were non-mukallaf, or a mahram relative of the victim, or if part of the caravan cuts [the road] against the other part, or if he cuts the road night or day within a city or two cities, he is not subjected to hadd, so the guardian exacts qisas or forgives) is an explanation of the cases in which there is no hadd, and they are six cases: The first: if he wounded and did not kill and did not take property, because there is no hadd in this offense, so the right of the slave appears, so he is subjected to qisas in what has qisas and takes compensation in what has compensation, and that is up to the guardians; so in al-Hidayah. And there is a consideration in it because that belongs to the wounded person, not his guardian, so if the wound led to death, the hadd should be obligatory, and when he took property...
Source pages p. 115

Commentary
This passage is a series of rulings on auxiliary points regarding highway robbery. First, it clarifies that after the hadd is carried out, the robber is not liable to return the property taken, similar to minor theft. The commentator suggests it would be better to say 'he is not liable for what he did' to include both property and life. Second, non-direct participants (accomplices) are treated the same as the direct participant in both taking and killing; the ruling applies to all even if only one of them committed the act, because the crime is waging war (muharaba). Third, any weapon is considered equal; even a stick or stone qualifies because they can cut off the road by harming passersby. Fourth, there is a fifth case: if the robber takes property and wounds someone, he is still amputated, and no compensation is required for the wound because the hadd (a divine right) supersedes the individual's right for the wound, similar to how the property is not returned. Fifth, there are six cases where the hadd of highway robbery is not applied: (1) if he wounds without killing or taking property (then it becomes a private matter of qisas or compensation); (2) if he kills only and then repents; (3) if some of the robbers are non-mukallaf (insane or minors); (4) if the robber is a mahram relative of the victim; (5) if part of the caravan preys on another part (i.e., within the same traveling group); (6) if the robbery occurs within a city or two cities (i.e., not on a highway). In these cases, the hadd is dropped, and the case becomes a private claim. The text begins discussing the first case and notes a critical point: the guardian (wali) is not the same as the wounded person; the right belongs to the wounded himself, not his guardian. If the wound leads to death, the hadd should apply. The passage is cut off while discussing the sixth case.

Key Terms
غير المباشر (ghayr al-mubashir): One who does not directly commit the act but participates in the robbery; treated equally in hadd.
محرم (mahram): A blood relative whom one cannot marry; if the robber is a mahram of the victim, the hadd is dropped.
مصر (misr): A city or town; if the robbery occurs within a city, it is not considered highway robbery (hirabah) and thus no hadd.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) The robber wounded only, without killing or taking property.
B) The robber used a stick instead of a sword.
C) The robber is a mahram relative of the victim.
D) The robbery occurs within a city.

Key Takeaway
In highway robbery, non-direct participants and any weapon are treated identically, but six specific exceptions drop the hadd and relegate the matter to private claims.

Crucifixion of the Highway Robber: Method and Intra-Hanafi Dispute
Describes the hadd punishment for a highway robber who kills, including crucifixion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the preferred Hanafi method of crucifixion for a highway robber who kills, distinguish it from the views of Abu Yusuf and al-Tahawi, and explain the rationale of tashhir and isti'jal al-mawt.

Core Idea
The highway robber who kills is crucified alive for three days — public exposure and hastening death.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Rā'iq details the hadd punishment for a highway robber who commits murder. It focuses on the method of crucifixion, explaining the preferred Hanafi position (crucify alive) and noting the differences of Abu Yusuf and al-Tahawi. The rationale is both deterrence through public exposure and mercy by hastening death.

Guided Path
1
Identify the crime and its hadd
The passage addresses a highway robber (muharib) who also kills. The hadd is death, regardless of the victim's guardian's pardon.
The punishment is fixed (hadd), not discretionary.
2
Understand the role of the victim's family
Even if the guardian pardons, the hadd is executed because it is a right of Allah.
This distinguishes hadd from qisas.
3
Analyze the method of crucifixion
The matn states the robber is crucified alive for three days, then his abdomen is stabbed with a spear until death.
The commentary explains 'yubba'j' means to slit open.
4
Note the intra-Hanafi differences
Tahawi: kill first, then crucify. Abu Yusuf: leave body on cross until it decomposes. The preferred view (al-ashah) is to crucify alive.
All three views are within the Hanafi school.
5
Grasp the rationale
Crucifying alive achieves public exposure (tashhir) as a deterrent and hastens death (isti'jal al-mawt) as a form of mercy.
The three-day limit avoids prolonged suffering and nuisance to people.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإن قتل قتل حداً وإن عفا الولى وإن قتل وأخذ قطع وقتل أو صلب أو قتل وصلب ويصلب حياً ثلاثة أيام ويبعج بطنه برمح حتى يموت ولم يضمن ما أخذ قوله : (ويصلب حياً ثلاثة أيام ويبعج بطنه برمح حتى يموت) تشهيراً له واستعجالاً لموته، ومعنى يبعج يشق ؛ كذا في المغرب. والصلب حياً ظاهر المذهب كما في المجتبى وهو الأصح. وعند الطحاوي أنه يقتل ثم يصلب. وقيد بالثلاثة لأنه لا يصلب أكثر منها توقياً عن تأذي الناس فإذا مضى له ثلاثة من وقت موته يخلي بينه وبين أهله ليدفنوه. وعن أبي يوسف أنه يترك على الخشبة حتى يتقطع فيسقط قوله : (ولم يضمن ما أخذ) يعني بعد ما أقيم عليه الحد كما في السرقة الصغرى ولو قال ولم يضمن ما فعل لكان أولى لأنه لا يضمن ما قتل وما جرح لذلك المعنى
and if he kills, he is killed as a hadd, even if the guardian pardons; and if he kills and takes (property), he is amputated and killed, or crucified, or killed and crucified. And he is crucified alive for three days and his belly is ripped open with a spear until he dies. And he is not liable for what he took. (Commentary: His saying: 'and he is crucified alive for three days and his belly is ripped open with a spear until he dies' is for public exposure and hastening his death, and the meaning of 'yubba'j' is to slit open; as in al-Mughrib. And crucifying alive is the apparent position of the madhhab as in al-Mujtaba, and it is the most correct. According to al-Tahawi, he is killed first then crucified. And it is restricted to three because he is not crucified for more than that to avoid harming people, so when three days pass from the time of his death, he is left for his family to bury him. And from Abu Yusuf that he is left on the wooden beam until he decomposes and falls. His saying: 'and he is not liable for what he took' means after the hadd is established on him, as in the lesser theft, and if he had said 'and he is not liable for what he did' it would be better because he is not liable for what he killed or wounded for that reason.
Source pages p. 115

Commentary
This passage presents the ruling for a highway robber who commits murder. The hadd punishment is death by execution, irrespective of whether the victim's guardian forgives. If the robber also takes property, the punishment includes amputation of the right hand and left foot (khilaf) along with death, and the judge may choose to crucify the robber. The commentary explains the method of crucifixion: the robber is crucified alive for three days, then his abdomen is stabbed with a spear until death. This is the relied-upon Hanafi position. There is a difference of opinion: Imam Tahawi holds that the robber should be killed first then crucified, while Abu Yusuf opines that the body should be left on the cross until it decomposes and falls. The text also clarifies that after the hadd is executed, the robber is not required to make financial compensation for the property taken, as the hadd replaces that liability. This is analogous to the lesser theft where the thief is not liable for the value after amputation.

Key Terms
يصلب حياً (yuslabu hayyan): Crucifixion while still alive, not after death; the criminal is tied to a wooden beam (or cross) while living.
يبعج (yubba'j): To slit open the abdomen or rip open the belly, here with a spear to cause death.
تشهير (tashhir): Public exposure and humiliation of the criminal as a deterrent.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Crucify alive for three days (the apparent madhhab).
B) Kill first, then crucify (Tahawi).
C) Leave on the cross until decomposition (Abu Yusuf).
D) All of the above are equally valid.

Key Takeaway
The highway robber who kills is crucified alive for three days as a hadd penalty, with amputation if property was taken; the preferred Hanafi view prioritises public deterrence and quick death.

Indirect Acts in Highway Robbery: Stick and Stone Like Sword
Ruling: indirect actors in highway robbery are like direct actors; stick/stone equal sword.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling that indirect participants in highway robbery are treated as direct participants and explain the rationale based on collective action.

Core Idea
In highway robbery, the indirect actor is like the direct actor—all share liability because the crime depends on mutual support.

This lesson clarifies a key principle in Hanafi hudud: in highway robbery (muharaba), every member of the group is held fully responsible for the acts of any one member, even if only one actually kills or takes property. This is because the crime is inherently collective—the group acts as a unified force, each supporting the others. We will also see that the means used—whether sword, stick, or stone—does not affect liability.

Guided Path
1
Identify the principle
The text states 'ghayr al-mubashir kalmubashir' (the indirect is like the direct). In the context of highway robbery, this means that an accomplice who does not directly kill or take is still fully liable.
The principle is specific to highway robbery; it does not apply to ordinary crimes.
2
Understand the rationale
Why are all participants liable? Because highway robbery (muharaba) is realized by the group acting in concert, each supporting the other (رداً للبعض). The collective nature makes every participant responsible for the outcome.
The condition is that the killing or taking is from one of them; the group's support makes it a joint act.
3
Apply the principle to means
The text equates a stick and stone to a sword. The essence of highway robbery is blocking the road (قطع الطريق), not the specific weapon. Therefore, even non-lethal tools make participants liable.
This reinforces that liability is based on participation in the crime, not on the method used.
4
Recognize the legal effect
The rulings (hadd punishments) apply to all participants based on the direct action of any one of them. If one kills, all are liable for the hadd of killing.
This is an exception to the general rule that only the direct actor is liable.
5
Note the scope
This principle is specific to highway robbery. In other crimes, such as murder or theft, only the direct actor is liable unless there is a conspiracy.
The Hanafi school treats highway robbery as a crime against public security, hence the collective liability.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (غير المباشر كالمباشر) يعني في الأخذ والقتل حتى تجري الأحكام على الكل بمباشرة البعض لأنه جزاء المحاربة وهي تتحقق بأن يكون البعض رداً للبعض حتى إذا زالت أقدامهم انحازوا اليهم وإنما الشرط القتل من واحد منهم وقد تحقق قوله: (والعصا والحجر (كالسيف لأنه يقع قطعاً للطريق بقطع المارة
His saying: (The indirect actor is like the direct actor) meaning in taking and killing, so that the rulings apply to all by the direct action of some, because it is the penalty for highway robbery, and it is realized by some being a support for others, so that when their feet slip, they rally to them. And the condition is only that the killing is from one of them, and it has been realized. His saying: (And the stick and the stone are like the sword) because they constitute blocking the road by blocking the passersby.
Source pages p. 115

Commentary
This passage establishes a key principle in the fiqh of highway robbery: all participants are treated as direct actors, even if only one actually carries out the killing or theft. This is because highway robbery is a collective crime where the group acts as a unified force. The commentary explains that the condition is that at least one of them kills, and then all are liable. Similarly, using a stick or stone to block the road is equivalent to using a sword, because the essence of the crime is blocking the road, not the specific weapon.

Key Terms
غير المباشر (ghayr al-mubashir): The indirect actor or accomplice who does not directly carry out the act but participates in the crime.
المباشر (al-mubashir): The direct actor who personally commits the act (e.g., killing or taking).
قطع الطريق (qat' al-tariq): Highway robbery, the act of blocking the road to rob or kill passersby.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the text, why are indirect actors in highway robbery treated like direct actors?
A) Because they agreed to commit the crime before the act.
B) Because highway robbery is a collective act where the group depends on each other.
C) Because they are all equally sinful.
D) Because the punishment is the same for all crimes.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, highway robbery is a collective crime: all participants are equally liable for the actions of any one member, and the means used (stick, stone, sword) do not affect liability.

Combined Theft and Wounding: Amputation Only, Wound Void
Ruling: in hirabah, amputation covers both theft and wounding; the wound claim becomes void.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling when a highway robber both steals property and wounds the victim, and explain the legal reason (talil) why the wound is not compensated.

Core Idea
In highway robbery, the hadd (amputation) eliminates both property and wound claims; the victim's right lapses because the public right of Allah takes precedence.

This passage from Bahr al-Raiq addresses a key scenario in the law of hirabah (highway robbery): when the robber both takes property and wounds someone. The ruling clarifies that only the hadd punishment (amputation) is applied, and no separate compensation is due for the wound. Understanding this ruling requires grasping the Hanafi principle that the right of Allah (hadd) overrides individual claims.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
Determine the scenario: the robber takes property and wounds a person. The ruling from the matn is that he is amputated (hand and leg from opposite sides) and the wound is void (no qisas or diya).
2
Locate the legal reasoning (talil)
The commentator explains that when the hadd (right of Allah) is established, the right of the servant (for the wound) falls. This is based on the precedence of divine right over personal claims.
The talil is explicit in the source: 'because when the hadd, which is the right of Allah, is established, the right of the servant falls'.
3
Examine the analogy drawn
The commentator analogizes the falling of the wound claim to the falling of the property claim: just as the hadd prevents recovery of stolen property, it also prevents compensation for injury. This analogy reinforces the principle.
4
Consider the implications
Note that this ruling is the mufta bihi position in the Zahir al-Riwayah. Cross-madhhab differences exist: some schools (e.g., Shafi'i) may require separate compensation for the wound. Understand the practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf): in Hanafi fiqh, the victim cannot sue for the wound.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإن أخذ مالاً وجرح قطع وبطل الجرح قوله : (وإن أخذ مالاً وجرح قطع وبطل الجرح) بيان للحالة الخامسة لهم وهي أن يأخذ المال ويجرح إنساناً فيقطع يده ورجله من خلاف ولا يجب شيء لأجل الجرح لأنه لما الحد حقاً الله تعالى سقطت وجب عصمة النفس حقاً للعبد كما تسقط عصمة المال .
And if he takes property and wounds, he is amputated and the wound is void. His saying: (And if he takes property and wounds, he is amputated and the wound is void) is an explanation of the fifth case for them, which is that he takes the property and wounds a person, so he is amputated (hand and leg from opposite sides) and nothing is due for the wound, because when the hadd, which is the right of Allah, is established, the right of the servant (for the wound) falls, just as the protection of property falls.
Source pages p. 115

Commentary
This passage discusses the fifth scenario in highway robbery: when the robber takes property and also wounds someone. The punishment is only the hadd amputation (cutting off the right hand and left foot from opposite sides). The wound is not compensated separately, whether by qisas or diya. The reason given is that the hadd (a right of Allah) takes precedence and causes the personal right (for the wound) to lapse, similar to how the right of the victim for the property is also waived by the hadd. This reflects the principle that in hudud, the public right overrides individual claims.

Key Terms
بطل الجرح (batala al-jarh): The wound becomes void in terms of liability; no qisas or diya is due because the hadd covers it.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) He is amputated and also must pay diya for the wound.
B) He is amputated only and the wound is not compensated.
C) He is killed only.
D) He is imprisoned and the property is returned.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, when a highway robber both steals and wounds, only the hadd amputation applies; the claim for the wound is void because the public right (hadd) overrides the private right.

Six Exceptions to the Hadd Penalty in Highway Robbery
Lists and explains six situations where the hadd for highway robbery is not applied.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the six exceptions to the hadd penalty for highway robbery and explain why each exception negates the penalty.

Core Idea
The hadd for highway robbery is waived in six specific cases due to the nature of the crime or the status of offenders/victims.

This lesson from 'Bahr al-Raiq' details six situations where the fixed penalty of amputation (hadd) for highway robbery (hiraba) is not applied. These exceptions arise when the crime lacks full conditions or when the perpetrators are not fully accountable. Understanding these exceptions is essential for the correct application of hudud punishments.

Guided Path
1
List the six exceptions
Identify each case enumerated in the text: wounding without killing/taking, killing then repenting, non-mukallaf robber, mahram victim, internal caravan attack, robbery inside a city.
Memorize these cases as they are frequently tested.
2
Determine the missing condition
For each exception, identify which condition of hadd is absent or why liability is reduced (e.g., no property taken, no nisab, lack of legal capacity, absence of highway element).
3
Note the consequences
When hadd is waived, the victim's private rights (qisas, compensation) remain accessible to the victim or their guardians.
The hadd is a public right; its waiver does not erase private claims.
4
Analyze the repentance dispute
Examine the internal Hanafi disagreement: does repentance alone drop the hadd, or must property also be returned? The relied-upon view is that the hadd drops.
Imam Muhammad al-Shaybani in al-Asl supports dropping.
5
Apply the principle of private rights
Understand that even when hadd is waived, the victim or guardians may still seek qisas or compensation, as the private right remains intact.

(Commentary) Explanation of the cases where there is no hadd, which are six: First: if he wounds without killing or taking property, because there is no hadd for this offense, so the right of the servant appears, and qisas is taken for what has qisas, and compensation is taken for what has compensation, and that is for the guardians (awliya); as in al-Hidayah. There is a consideration because that is for the wounded person himself, not his guardian, and if the wound leads to death, the hadd should be required... (continues) His saying: (and if he said ...) said in al-Nahr: because when he explained that his killing is in return for the killing of an inviolable soul and wounding it, perhaps it might be imagined that property is taken from his estate since it was not met with something, so he clarified that he is not liable for it... His saying: (there is a consideration...) said al-Maqdisi: by guardians (awliya) is meant that which includes the wounded person himself, so he is his own guardian if he is eligible, otherwise his father or executor etc. His saying: (it is fitting that the hadd should be required) meaning just as if he only killed and it becomes the third case... His saying: (then the answer is that their intention...) said al-Maqdisi... The effective cause for the hadd here is the nisab (minimum value), so taking less than the nisab is tantamount to nothing, so if he takes less than nisab and wounds, it falls under his saying 'and if he only wounds'. Similarly, if he takes property for which amputation is prescribed (like perishable items). The commentator said: And if this taking was killing, the hadd would not be required also with a defect... Second: if he kills and then repents before being captured, there is no hadd because this offense is not established after repentance due to the exception mentioned in the text, or because repentance depends on returning the property, and there is no amputation in its like, so the right of the servant appears in life and property until the guardian takes qisas or forgives, and he must guarantee if the property is destroyed or consumed; as in al-Hidayah. Third and fourth: if some of the robbers are not legally responsible like a child or insane, or are a mahram relative of the victim, then the hadd is dropped for all, because it is a single offense established by all, so if the act of some is not a cause (for the hadd), the others' act is part of the cause, and the ruling is not established, so it is like the mistaken with the intentional. Fifth and sixth: (are mentioned)... And the answer is that when killing is isolated, the law prescribes the hadd for it, so we know that the law made their killing a cause for property by legal fiction, and when it is accompanied by taking property, it is looked at because it is the intention, so if it is little, it prevents the hadd, and if it is much, it does not prevent. His saying: (until the guardian takes qisas) said in al-Fath: then it is necessary that the killing be with iron or the like, because qisas is only required with that according to Abu Hanifah.
Source pages p. 115

Commentary
This lesson presents six cases where the hadd punishment for highway robbery is not applied. The first case is when the robber only wounds (without killing or taking property); then the hadd is waived, and the victim can claim qisas or diya. The text notes a criticism: if the wound leads to death, hadd should apply. The second case is when the robber repents before being captured: the hadd is waived, but the victim's rights for qisas and property remain. There is a difference of opinion on whether repentance alone suffices if property is not returned. The third and fourth cases: if some of the robbers are non-mukallaf (e.g., minors or insane) or the victim is a mahram relative, the hadd is waived for all, because the crime is a single act; the presence of such a person in the group disrupts the condition for hadd. The fifth and sixth cases: if one part of the caravan attacks another (internal robbery) or if the robbery occurs in a city (at night or day), the hadd is also waived because the element of highway (outside city) is missing. The commentary also discusses the issue of taking less than nisab (minimum value), treating it as no taking, so it falls under the first case. The text emphasizes that these rulings are based on the definition of highway robbery and the nisab requirement.

Key Terms
غير مكلف (ghayr mukallaf): A person who is not legally responsible due to lack of puberty or sanity (e.g., child, insane person).
ذا رحم محرم (dhu rahm mahram): A mahram relative (unmarriageable kin) of the victim, e.g., father, son, brother, uncle, etc.
النصاب (al-nisab): The minimum value (nisab) of property required for the hadd of amputation in theft and highway robbery.

Key Takeaway
The hadd for highway robbery is not applied in six specific cases, but private rights such as qisas and compensation remain.

intermediate
6 min
Ruling on Taking Below Nisab with Wounding or Killing
States the ruling when stolen amount is below nisab and there is wounding or killing

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling for highway robbery when the stolen property is below nisab and explain why adding wounding or killing does not trigger the hadd.

Core Idea
Property below nisab is legally irrelevant; even with killing, the hadd for theft is not applied.

This lesson explores a nuanced ruling in Hanafi fiqh: when highway robbers take property below the nisab threshold and also wound or kill, the hadd punishment for theft is not applied. The key is that the nisab condition is a prerequisite for the hadd, and the robbers' primary intent is considered to be the property, even if violence occurs.

Guided Path
1
Identify the nisab condition
The nisab for theft is 10 dirhams (or 1/4 dinar). If the stolen property is below this value, the hadd for theft is not triggered, regardless of accompanying acts.
2
Understand the ruling for wounding
If a robber takes property below nisab and wounds the victim, the act is treated as if only wounding occurred. The hadd for theft is not applied.
The wounding may be punished under ta'zir or qisas, not hadd.
3
Extend to killing
Similarly, if the robber kills the victim while taking property below nisab, the hadd for theft is still not applied. The killing does not trigger the hadd either, because the primary intent is deemed to be the property.
This is a counterintuitive ruling: adding killing actually reduces the punishment.
4
Analyze the objection and response
The text raises an objection: if killing alone carries hadd, why does killing combined with taking property not carry hadd? The answer: the robbers' dominant intent is the property, not the killing. Thus, we look to the property, which is below nisab.
If they had confined themselves to killing without taking property, their intent is murder, and the hadd would apply.
5
Note the strange nature of the ruling
The commentator remarks that this is a strange and amazing case because increasing the crime (adding property) results in a lighter punishment. This is recorded in al-Fawa'id al-Zahiriyyah for preservation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وهي هو النصاب كان أخذ ما دونه بمنزلة العدم، فإذا أخذ ما دون النصاب وجرح فهو داخل تحت قوله وإن جرح فقط». وكذا إذا أخذ مالاً يقطع فيه كالأشياء التي يتسارع إليها الفساد. قال الشارح: ولو كان هذا الأخذ قتل لا يجب الحد أيضاً مع طعن فإنه قال : القتل وحده يوجب عیسی الزيادة؟ فجوابه أن قصدهم المال غالباً فينظر إليه لا غير بخلاف ما إذا اقتصروا على القتل لأنه تبين أن مقصدهم القتل دون المال فيحدون فعدت هذه من الغرائب وأمر بحفظها في الفوائد الظهيرية، وعدها من أعجب المسائل من حيث إن ازدياد الجناية أورث الخفة
And it (the nisab) is the threshold; taking less than it is like nothing. So if he takes less than nisab and wounds, it falls under his statement: 'and if he wounds only'. Similarly, if he takes property for which cutting is prescribed, like things that quickly spoil. The commentator said: And if this taking was killing, the hadd is not obligatory either, with a challenge. Because he said: 'Killing alone necessitates hadd, so how can it be prevented with the addition of taking property?'. The answer: Their intent is mostly the property, so only that is considered, unlike if they confined themselves to killing, because it becomes clear that their intent was killing without property, so they are subject to hadd. This is counted among the strange issues, and commanded to be preserved in al-Fawa'id al-Zahiriyyah, and counted among the most amazing issues because the increase in crime caused lightness.
Source pages p. 116

Commentary
This passage discusses a nuanced ruling: if a highway robber takes property below the nisab threshold and also wounds, it is treated as if he only wounded (i.e., no hadd for theft). Similarly, if he takes perishable goods that are subject to cutting, the same applies. The commentator then raises an objection: if killing alone carries the hadd, why does killing combined with taking property not carry it? The answer is that the robbers' primary intent is property, so we look to that; however, if they confine themselves to killing without taking property, their intent is murder, and the hadd applies. This is a strange case because increasing the crime (adding property) actually reduces the punishment.

Key Terms
الحد (al-hadd): The fixed legal punishment prescribed by divine law for specific crimes such as theft and highway robbery.
النصاب (al-nisab): The minimum value of stolen property that triggers the hadd of theft, set at 10 dirhams in the Hanafi school.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, if a highway robber takes property worth 5 dirhams (below nisab) and wounds the victim, what is the ruling?
A) He is subject to hadd for theft and wounding.
B) He is subject to hadd for wounding only, as the property is below nisab.
C) No hadd applies at all.
D) He is subject to hadd for killing only.

Key Takeaway
The hadd of theft requires the stolen property to reach nisab; if it does not, even accompanying wounding or killing does not trigger the hadd, as the primary intent is the property.

Repentance Before Capture in Highway Robbery
Ruling that repentance before capture removes hadd for highway robbery

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on repentance before capture in highway robbery and explain the evidence from the Quran and the intra-madhhab disagreement.

Core Idea
Repentance before capture drops the hadd but preserves the victim's rights – the text explicitly excludes.

This lesson addresses the second case in the chapter on highway robbery: what if the robber kills and then repents before capture? The Hanafi ruling is that the hadd punishment is not applied, based on the exception in Surah al-Ma'idah. However, the victim's heirs retain the right to qisas or diya, and the robber must guarantee the property. We'll explore the proof and a difference among Hanafi scholars.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and ruling
The act is highway robbery involving murder, followed by repentance before capture. The ruling is that no hadd is applied.
The key temporal condition: repentance before capture.
2
Locate the dalil
The Quranic exception in 5:34 is cited: except those who repent before you overpower them.
The verse creates an explicit exception from the prescribed punishment.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
The exception clause directly excludes those who repent before capture from the hadd punishment, indicating that repentance nullifies the state's right to punish.
But it does not affect private rights like qisas.
4
Note the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf
Does repentance alone drop hadd, or must property also be returned? Imam Muhammad is reported to say repentance suffices; others require return.
The mufta bihi position is that return is not strictly required for dropping hadd, but it is part of complete repentance.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثانية لو قتل فتاب قبل الأخذ لا حد لأن هذه الجناية لا تقام بعد التوبة للاستثناء المذكور في النص، أو لأن التوبة تتوقف على رد المال ولا قطع في مثله فظهر حق العبد في النفس والمال حتى يستوفي الولي القصاص أو يعفو، ويجب الضمان إذا هلك في يده أو استهلكه؛ كذا في الهداية. وإنما قيد بالمختص بالقتل ليعلم حكم أخذ المال بالأولى. وفي المبسوط والمحيط : رد المال من تمام توبتهم لتنقطع خصومة صاحبه، ولو تاب ولم يرد المال لم يذكره في الكتاب واختلفوا فيه؛ فقيل لا يسقط الحد كسائر الحدود لا تسقط بالتوبة وقيل يسقط أشار إليه محمد في الأصل.
Second: If he killed and then repented before being captured, no hadd, because this crime is not carried out after repentance due to the exception mentioned in the text, or because repentance depends on returning the property, and there is no cutting in such a case. So the right of the servant appears in the life and property until the wali exacts retaliation or forgives, and guarantee is obligatory if it perishes in his hand or he consumes it; this is in al-Hidayah. It is restricted to the one specified for killing to teach the ruling of taking property a fortiori. In al-Mabsut and al-Muhit: Returning the property completes their repentance to terminate the dispute of its owner. If he repented but did not return the property, it is not mentioned in the book, and they differed: some said the hadd is not dropped as other hadd punishments are not dropped by repentance; some said it is dropped, indicated by Muhammad in al-Asl.
Source pages p. 116

Commentary
This lesson covers the second issue: if a highway robber kills and repents before capture, the hadd (capital punishment) is not applied. The proof cited is the exception in the Quranic verse on highway robbery (Surah al-Ma'idah 5:33-34) which exempts those who repent before being overpowered. Alternatively, repentance requires returning the stolen property, and since the property was not taken or is lost, the hadd for theft is not relevant. However, the right of the victim (or his heir) remains: they may seek qisas or forgive. Additionally, the robber must guarantee the property if it was destroyed or consumed. The passage notes a difference of opinion within the Hanafi school: whether the hadd drops if the robber repents but does not return the property. Imam Muhammad (al-Shaybani) is reported to say it does drop, while others say it does not.

Key Terms
التوبة (al-tawbah): Repentance from sin, which in this context must include returning stolen property and ceasing the crime.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the primary evidence for dropping the hadd for a highway robber who repents before capture?
A) Consensus of the Companions.
B) The Quranic exception in Surah al-Ma'idah 5:34.
C) The practice of the Rightly-Guided Caliphs.
D) Analogy to repentance in theft.

Key Takeaway
Repentance before capture removes the hadd for highway robbery, but the victim's heirs retain the right to qisas and the robber must guarantee the property.

Effect of Non-Mukallaf or Mahram Among Highway Robbers
Ruling on collective liability in highway robbery with non-mukallaf/mahram participants.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on hadd when a highway robbery group includes a non-mukallaf or mahram participant and explain the qiyas reasoning using the collective crime principle.

Core Idea
Hadd drops for ALL robbers if any participant is non-mukallaf or mahram — the crime is one collective act.

This lesson examines a unique Hanafi ruling on collective criminal liability. When a group commits highway robbery, the act is treated as a single crime. If one participant is legally non-responsible (child, insane) or is a mahram relative of the victim, the entire hadd punishment falls away. The reasoning is based on qiyas to the case of intention mixed with mistake, and on the unity of the protective space (hirz). Understanding this ruling clarifies how the Hanafi school prioritizes the integrity of the collective act over individual desert.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling
When some highway robbers are non-mukallaf (child/insane) or mahram relative of the victim, the hadd of amputation is dropped for all participants.
2
Recognize the underlying principle
Highway robbery is a single collective crime (jinaiya wahida). The hadd depends on a complete cause (sabab) formed by the entire group.
3
Trace the qiyas (analogy)
The text analogizes to a group where some act mistakenly: the intentional ones are not treated as intentional because the act is mixed. Here, the non-responsible or mahram participant prevents the complete cause, so hadd is not established for anyone.
The analogy is qiyas shabahah: the deficiency in one part affects the whole.
4
Distinguish from exceptions
If a musta'min (protected non-Muslim) is among the robbers, the hadd drops only for that individual, not for others, because the defect is in his personal immunity (isma), not in the collective act.
The musta'min's situation is different because the defect is personal, not collective.
5
Apply further ramifications
When hadd drops, the victim's heirs retain the right to qisas (if death occurred) or forgiveness. For slaves, the master pays ransom; women pay diya for the hand.
These are alternative consequences after hadd is prevented.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثالثة والرابعة لو كان بعض القطاع غير مكلف كالصبي والمجنون أو ذا رحم محرم من المقطوع عليه فإن القطع يسقط عن الكل لأنها جناية واحدة قامت بالكل، فإذا لم يقع فعل بعضهم موجباً كان فعل الباقين بعض العلة وبه لا يثبت الحكم فصار كالخاطئ مع العامد. أطلق في ذي الرحم المحرم فشمل ما إذا لم يكن مشتركاً بين المقطوع عليهم وهو الأصح لأن الجناية واحدة فالامتناع في حق البعض يوجب الامتناع في حق الباقين بخلاف ما إذا كان فيهم مستأمن لأن الامتناع في حقه لخلل في ا العصمة وهو يخصه، أما هنا الامتناع لخلل في الحرز والقافلة حرز واحد، وإذا سقط الحد صار القتل إلى الأولياء لظهور حق العبد على ما ذكرنا، وإن شاؤوا قتلوه وإن شاؤوا عفوا . وأشار بذي الرحم المحرم إلى أنه لو كان في المقطوع عليهم شريك مفاوض لبعض القطاع لا يحدون كذي الرحم المحرم. وفي المبسوط : تابوا وفيهم عبد قطع دفعه مولاه أو فداه كما لو فعله في غير قطع الطريق، وهذا لأنه لا قصاص بين العبيد والأحرار فيما دون النفس فيبقى حكم الدفع والفداء، فإن كانت فيهم امرأة فعلت ذلك فعليها دية اليد في مالها لأنه لا قصاص بين الرجال والنساء في الأطراف والواقع منها عمداً لا تعقله العاقلة
Third and Fourth: If some of the highway robbers are not legally responsible (mukallaf) like a child or insane person, or a mahram relative of the victim, then the cutting (hadd) is dropped from all, because it is a single crime committed by the group. If the act of some does not carry a legal consequence, the act of the others is only part of the cause, and the ruling is not established. It is like the mistaken person with the intentional. The statement about mahram is absolute, so it includes even if the mahram is not a partner among the victims; this is the most correct because the crime is one, so the prevention in the right of some necessitates prevention in the right of the others, unlike if there is a musta'min among them, because the prevention in his right is due to a defect in his protection (isma), which is specific to him. Here the prevention is due to a defect in the hira (the caravan is one hira). When the hadd falls, the killing becomes subject to the heirs due to the appearance of the right of the servant as we mentioned; if they wish they kill him, if they wish they forgive. He indicated by 'mahram' that if among the victims there is a sharik mufawid of some of the robbers, they are not punished like the mahram. In al-Mabsut: If they repented and among them is a slave who committed the cutting, his master pays or ransoms him as if done outside highway robbery, because there is no qisas between slaves and freemen for below the soul, so the ruling of payment and ransom remains. If there is a woman among them who did that, she must pay the diya of the hand from her own wealth because there is no qisas between men and women for limbs, and the intentional act is not borne by the 'aqila.
Source pages p. 116

Commentary
This lesson covers the third and fourth issues regarding highway robbery: if some of the robbers are not legally responsible (child, insane) or are mahram relatives of the victim, the hadd of amputation is dropped for all the robbers. The reasoning is that the crime of highway robbery is a single collective act. If the act of one participant does not legally count (due to lack of responsibility or a relationship that prevents punishment), then the act of the others becomes an incomplete cause (ba'd al-illa), and the hadd cannot be established. This is analogous to a case where one person acts intentionally and another by mistake—the whole is not treated as intentional. The text distinguishes this from the case where a musta'min (protected non-Muslim) is among the robbers; there, the defect is in his personal immunity, not in the collective act. When the hadd drops, the victim's heirs retain the right to qisas (if death occurred) or forgiveness. The passage also mentions additional rulings: if a slave is among the robbers, his master may ransom him; if a woman is involved, she pays diya for the hand (since no qisas between genders for limbs).

Key Terms
المكلف (al-mukallaf): A person legally responsible: an adult, sane Muslim who understands obligations.
الحرز (al-hirz): A secure place that protects property; here, the caravan itself is considered a single hirz.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Only the child escapes hadd; the adult is still liable.
B) The hadd is dropped for both the child and the adult.
C) The adult is subject to hadd but the child is not.
D) The child is subject to ta'zir and the adult to hadd.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, highway robbery is a single collective act; if any participant is non-mukallaf or mahram, the hadd of amputation is dropped for all participants.
